10027{_}1266{_}Noaks certainly seemed to have discovered a chum in the black sheep of the Upper Fourth, and the Triple Alliance began to congratulate themselves that he would trouble them no further.
10027{_}1267{_}In a big school like Ronleigh College there was plenty of room for everybody to go his own way without fear of running his head into people whom he wished to avoid.
10027{_}1268{_}Our three friends, however, seemed fated to find in the person of Noaks junior a perpetual stumbling-block and cause of disquietude and annoyance.
10027{_}1269{_}They had no sooner succeeded in setting him at a distance when an incident occurred which brought them once more into violent collision with the enemy.
10027{_}1270{_}The pavilion, which has already been mentioned as standing on the match ground, was a handsome wooden structure, surrounded by some low palings, in front of which was a small oblong patch of gravel.
10027{_}1271{_}On the second Saturday morning of the term Noaks and Mouler were lounging across this open space, when Oaks, the prefect, emerged from the pavilion, carrying in his hand a pot of paint he had been mixing for the goal-posts, which were just being put up.
10027{_}1272{_}On reaching the paling he suddenly ejaculated, "Bother!
10027{_}1273{_}I've forgotten the brush;" and resting the can on the top of the little gate-post, hurried back up the short flight of steps, and disappeared through the open door.
10027{_}1274{_}"I say, there's a good cock-shy," said Noaks, nodding his head in the direction of the paint.
10027{_}1275{_}"Umph!
10027{_}1276{_}shouldn't like to try," answered Mouler.
10027{_}1277{_}"Why not?"
10027{_}1278{_}"Because Oaks would jolly well punch both our heads."
10027{_}1279{_}"Well, here's a new kid coming; let's set him on to do it.
10027{_}1280{_}You speak to him; he knows me.
10027{_}1281{_}His name's Mugford."
10027{_}1282{_}The two cronies both picked up a handful of stones, and began throwing at the can, taking good care that their shots should fly wide of the mark.
10027{_}1283{_}Mugford, who, as we have already seen, was not blessed with the sharpest of wits, paused for a moment to watch the contest.
10027{_}1284{_}The paint had been mixed in an old fruit-tin, and at first sight it certainly seemed to have been put on the post for the sole purpose of being knocked off again.
10027{_}1285{_}"Hullo, you new kid!"
10027{_}1286{_}exclaimed Mouler.
10027{_}1287{_}"Look here, we want a chap for the third eleven next season--a fellow who can throw straight.
10027{_}1288{_}Come along, and let's see if you can hit that old can."
10027{_}1289{_}It certainly looked easy enough, and Mugford, pleased at being taken some notice of by a boy in the Upper Fourth, picked up some pebbles, and joined in the bombardment.
10027{_}1290{_}The second shot brought the tin down with a great clatter, and a flood of white paint spread all over the trim little pathway.
10027{_}1291{_}At the same instant Oaks dashed down the steps boiling with rage.
10027{_}1292{_}"Confound you!"
10027{_}1293{_}he cried; "who did that ?"
10027{_}1294{_}"I did," answered Mugford, half crying; "I thought it was empty."
10027{_}1295{_}"Thought it was empty!
10027{_}1296{_}why didn't you look, you young blockhead?"
10027{_}1297{_}cried the prefect, catching the small boy by the arm, while Noaks and Mouler burst into a roar of laughter.
10027{_}1298{_}Things would probably have gone hard with the unfortunate Mugford if at that moment a fifth party had not arrived on the scene.
10027{_}1299{_}The new-comer, who, from the show of whisker at the side of his face and the tone of authority in which he spoke, seemed to be one of the masters, was tall and muscular, with the bronze of a season's cricketing still upon his cheeks and neck.
10027{_}1300{_}"Stop a minute, Oaks," he said.
10027{_}1301{_}"I happened to see this little game; let's hear what the kid's got to say for himself."
10027{_}1302{_}In faltering tones Mugford told his story.
10027{_}1303{_}Without a word the stranger stepped up to Mouler and dealt him a sounding box on the ear.
10027{_}1304{_}"There!"
10027{_}1305{_}he said, "take that for your trouble; and now cut off down town and buy a fresh pot of paint out of your own pocket, and do it jolly quick, too.--As for you," he added, turning to Noaks, "get a spade out of that place under the pavilion and clean up this path.
10027{_}1306{_}If you weren't a new fellow I'd serve you the same.
10027{_}1307{_}Look out in future."
10027{_}1308{_}"And you look out too," muttered Noaks, glancing at Mugford with a fierce expression on his face as the two seniors moved off, "you beastly young sneak.
10027{_}1309{_}The first chance I get I'll give you the best licking you ever had in your life."
10027{_}1310{_}"Old Mug is rather a fool," remarked Jack Vance to Diggory a few hours later; "he ought to have seen through that.
10027{_}1311{_}But we must stand by him because of the Triple Alliance.
10027{_}1312{_}Noaks is sure to try to set on him the first chance he gets."
10027{_}1313{_}"Yes," answered Diggory; "look out for squalls."
10027{_}1314{_}CHAPTER VIII.
10027{_}1315{_}THIRD FORM ORATORY.
10027{_}1316{_}At the end of the first fortnight our three friends had begun to find their feet at Ronleigh, and the sense of being "outsiders" in everything was gradually wearing off as they grew more intimate with their schoolfellows.
10027{_}1317{_}Jack Vance and Diggory soon became popular members of "The Happy Family," and their loyalty to Mugford caused the latter's path to be much smoother than it probably would have been had he been compelled to tread it alone.
10027{_}1318{_}Carton turned out a capital fellow; Rathson, the small, sandy-haired boy mentioned in the previous chapter, and who generally went by the name of "Rats," took a great fancy to Jack; while Maxton repeated his assertion that young Trevanock was "the right sort," and as a further mark of his favour presented the new-comer with a moleskin of his own curing, which looked very nice, but, as "Rats" put it, "smelt rather fruity."
10027{_}1319{_}But it was not in the Third Form only that Diggory began to find friends; for by a lucky chance he was fortunate enough to make a good impression on the minds of the great men, who, as a rule, took no further notice of the small fry than to exact from them a certain amount of obedience, or in default a certain number of lines or other "impots."
10027{_}1320{_}One morning, soon after breakfast, a little group was gathered round Carton's desk in the big school-room, discussing the value of some foreign stamps, when a small boy came up to them, saying,--  "Is Trevanock here?
10027{_}1321{_}Well, Acton wants you now at once in his study."
10027{_}1322{_}"Hullo," said Carton, looking up from the sheet of specimens in front of him--"hullo, Diggy!
10027{_}1323{_}What have you been up to?"
10027{_}1324{_}"I haven't been doing anything," answered the other.
10027{_}1325{_}"What do you think he wants me for?"
10027{_}1326{_}"I don't know, but it sounds rather like getting a licking.
10027{_}1327{_}At all events, you'd better hurry up; prefects don't thank you for keeping them waiting.
10027{_}1328{_}His is the third door on the right as you go down the passage."
10027{_}1329{_}Diggory hastened to obey the summons, wondering what it could mean.
10027{_}1330{_}He found the door, and in answer to the loud "Come in!"
10027{_}1331{_}which greeted his knock turned the handle, and found himself for the first time inside one of the Sixth Form studies.
10027{_}1332{_}It was a small, square room, and looked very cosy and comfortable with its red window-curtains, well-filled bookshelf, and many little knick-knacks that adorned the walls and mantelpiece.
10027{_}1333{_}An array of silver cups, several photographs of cricket and football teams, and a miscellaneous pile of bats, fencing-sticks, Indian clubs, etc., standing in one corner, all spoke of the athlete; while carelessly thrown down on the top of a cupboard was an article for the possession of which many a, boy would have bartered the whole of his worldly wealth--a bit of worn blue velvet and the tarnished remnant of what had once been a gold tassel--the "footer cap" of Ronleigh College.
10027{_}1334{_}But it was not so much the furniture as the occupants of the study that attracted Diggory's attention.
10027{_}1335{_}John Acton, a tall, wiry fellow, who looked as though his whole body was as hard and tough as whip-cord, was standing leaning on the end of the mantelpiece talking to another of the seniors, who sat sprawling in a folding-chair on the other side of the fire; while seated at the table, turning over the leaves of what appeared to be a big manuscript book, was no less a personage than Allingford, the school captain.
10027{_}1336{_}"I don't understand a bit what's coming to 'Thirsty,'" the football leader was saying.
10027{_}1337{_}"I was rather chummy with him when we were in the Fifth, and he was all right then, but now he seems to be running to seed as fast as he can; and I believe it's a great deal that fellow Fletcher.--Hullo, youngster!
10027{_}1338{_}what d'you want?"
10027{_}1339{_}"I was told you wanted to see me," said Diggory nervously.
10027{_}1340{_}"Oh yes.
10027{_}1341{_}You were at The Birches, that school near Chatford, weren't you?
10027{_}1342{_}Well, I want to hear about that love affair my young brother had with the old chap's daughter.--It was an awful joke," added the speaker, addressing his companions.
10027{_}1343{_}"He was about fourteen, and she's a grown-up woman; and he was awfully gone, I can tell you.--How did he pop the question?"
10027{_}1344{_}"He wrote," answered Diggory.
10027{_}1345{_}"We tossed up whether he should do that or speak."
10027{_}1346{_}There was a burst of laughter.
10027{_}1347{_}"Did you see the letter?"
10027{_}1348{_}"Yes."
10027{_}1349{_}"What did he say?"
10027{_}1350{_}"I can't tell you."
10027{_}1351{_}"Why not?
10027{_}1352{_}don't you remember?"
10027{_}1353{_}"Yes; but he only showed me the letter on condition I wouldn't ever tell any one what was in it."
10027{_}1354{_}"Oh, that's all rot!
10027{_}1355{_}you can tell me; I'm his brother.
10027{_}1356{_}Come, out with it."
10027{_}1357{_}It was an awful thing to beard the lion in his den--for a new boy to face so great a personage as the football captain, and refuse point-blank to do as he was told.
10027{_}1358{_}Diggory shifted uneasily from one foot to another, and then glancing up he became aware of the fact that Allingford was gazing at him across the table with a curious expression, which somehow gave him fresh encouragement to persist in his refusal to disclose the contents of his former friend's love-letter.
10027{_}1359{_}"I can't tell you," he repeated; "it was a promise, you know."
10027{_}1360{_}The Ronleigh captain laughed.
10027{_}1361{_}"Well done," he said.
10027{_}1362{_}"I wish some other fellows were a bit more careful to keep their promises.--Acton, you beggar, you swore you'd keep up this register for me, and there's nothing entered for last term."
10027{_}1363{_}"Oh, bother you, Ally!"
10027{_}1364{_}exclaimed the other; "what a nigger-driver you are!--Hullo, there's the bell!--Here, kid, stick those two oranges in your pocket; go 'long!"
10027{_}1365{_}Diggory left the room, having gained something else besides the two oranges; for as he closed the door Allingford laughed again, and rising from his chair said, "He's a stanch little beggar; I think I'll keep an eye on him."
10027{_}1366{_}The subject of this remark hurried away, and had just joined the crowd of boys who were thronging into the big school for assembly, when some one took hold of his arm, and glancing round he was startled to see Jack Vance, looking very excited and dishevelled, and mopping his mouth with a blood-stained handkerchief.
10027{_}1367{_}"I say," exclaimed the latter, "have you seen Mugford?"
10027{_}1368{_}"No.
10027{_}1369{_}What's the matter?
10027{_}1370{_}what have you done to your mouth?"
10027{_}1371{_}"Why, I've had a beastly row with Noaks.
10027{_}1372{_}I'll 'tell you after school."
10027{_}1373{_}"No, tell me now," cried Diggory, pulling his companion aside into a corner by the door.
10027{_}1374{_}"Quick--what was it?"
10027{_}1375{_}"Why, he pounced down on Mugford, out there by the fives-court, and began twisting his arm and saying he'd pay him out for that paint-pot business.
10027{_}1376{_}I went to the rescue, and the beast hit me with the back of his hand here on the mouth.
10027{_}1377{_}I told him he was a cad, and said something about his father being only a man-servant, and having stolen our things.
10027{_}1378{_}I'm sorry now, for it was rather a low thing to do, but I was in such a wax I didn't think what I was saying.
10027{_}1379{_}Mouler was standing by, and he heard it, and laughed; and Noaks looked as if he'd have killed me.
10027{_}1380{_}I believe he would have knocked me down, only Rowlands, the prefect, came up and stopped the row."
10027{_}1381{_}There was no time for any further details, and the two boys had to rush away to their seats in order to escape being marked as late.
10027{_}1382{_}One thing was certain--that the Triple Alliance were once more embroiled in a quarrel with their ancient foe the former leader of the Philistines, and they knew enough of their adversary's character to feel sure that he would not pass over an event of this kind without some attempt at revenge.
10027{_}1383{_}It is probable that, if this had happened at Horace House, Jack Vance would have received a good licking as soon as the classes were dismissed; but a few very plain and forcible words spoken by Rowlands on the subject of knocking small boys about caused Noaks to postpone his retaliation.
10027{_}1384{_}"Look here," he said, meeting Jack Vance in the quadrangle during the interval: "just you keep your mouth shut about me and my father.
10027{_}1385{_}I've got two or three accounts to settle with you chaps already; just mind what you're up to."
10027{_}1386{_}He clinched his fist as though about to strike, then, with an ugly scowl, turned on his heel and walked away.
10027{_}1387{_}It must have been about three days after this encounter with Noaks that our three friends were called upon to attend a mass meeting of the Third Form, to consider the advisability of starting a periodical in opposition to the school magazine.
10027{_}1388{_}Important events connected with a later period of their life at Ronleigh render it necessary that we should not linger too long over the account of their first term; but some mention, however brief, should certainly be made of the memorable gathering to which we have referred.
10027{_}1389{_}A notice pinned on to the black-board, and pulled down as soon as Mr. Watford entered the classroom, announced the project in the following words:--                              "NOTICE."
10027{_}1390{_}"A meeting will be held in the 'old lab' directly          after dinner to-day, to make plans for starting a          magazine in opposition to _The Ronleian_.
10027{_}1391{_}All members of the Third Form are specially requested          to attend."
10027{_}1392{_}"FLETCHER II."
10027{_}1393{_}"J.
10027{_}1394{_}A.
10027{_}1395{_}BIBBS."
10027{_}1396{_}"You must come," said "Rats" to Diggory; "it'll be an awful lark."
10027{_}1397{_}"But what's it all about?"
10027{_}1398{_}"Oh, you'll hear when you get there.
10027{_}1399{_}It's Fletcher's idea; he wants to start a new magazine.
10027{_}1400{_}Eastfield, who edits _The Ronleian_, is Maxton's cousin; so Maxton's going to interrupt and get some other fellows to do the same.
10027{_}1401{_}I'm going to be part of the opposition," added the youthful "Rats," beaming with delight, "and I have got a whole heap of paper bags I'm going to burst while Fletcher's speaking."
10027{_}1402{_}The "old lab," as it was called, was a small brick building which stood on one side of the asphalt playground.
10027{_}1403{_}A new laboratory having recently been fitted up elsewhere, the former one was, for the time being, unused.
10027{_}1404{_}It was not more than about fifteen feet long by seven or eight feet wide; and as "The Happy Family" mustered in force, the place was crowded to overflowing.
10027{_}1405{_}The door having been closed, Fletcher Two mounted a low stone sink which ran along the end wall, and from this ready-made platform commenced to address the assembly :--  "Gentlemen,--We've met here, as you know, to talk over starting a fresh magazine.
10027{_}1406{_}_The Ronleian_ is a beastly swindle, and it's high time we had something different."
10027{_}1407{_}(A voice, "No, 'tisn't," and the bursting of a paper bag.)
10027{_}1408{_}"You shut up there!
10027{_}1409{_}I say it is a swindle: they didn't give any account of that fourth eleven match against Robertson's second, and they made fun of us in the 'Quad Gossip,' and said that in 'The Happy Family' there was a preponderance of monkey."
10027{_}1410{_}("So there is, and you're it!"
10027{_}1411{_}Laughter and another explosion.)
10027{_}1412{_}"What I propose is that we start a manuscript magazine for the Third Form, and that every fellow promise to take that, and never to buy a copy of the other.
10027{_}1413{_}We might pass it round, and charge a penny each to look at it.
10027{_}1414{_}Will you all subscribe?"
10027{_}1415{_}No one spoke, the silence only being broken by the sound of "Rats" blowing up another bag, which caused a fresh burst of laughter.
10027{_}1416{_}"Will you all subscribe?"
10027{_}1417{_}once more demanded the speaker.
10027{_}1418{_}There were mingled cries of "Yes!"
10027{_}1419{_}and "No!"
10027{_}1420{_}and a stentorian yell of "No, you cuckoo!
10027{_}1421{_}of course we won't," from Maxton, and another explosion.
10027{_}1422{_}"Look here, young 'Rats,' if you burst any more of those bags I'll come down and burst your head.--I forgot to say, gentlemen, that Mr. Bibbs has promised to assist in editing the paper; and I will now call upon him to give you an account of what it will contain."
10027{_}1423{_}Bibbs, the Third Form genius, was regarded by every one as a huge joke, and the very mention of his name caused a fresh burst of merriment.
10027{_}1424{_}He was a sad-faced, untidy-looking boy, quick and clever enough in some things, and equally dull and stupid in others.
10027{_}1425{_}The announcement that he would address the meeting had no sooner been made than half a dozen willing pairs of hands seized and hoisted him on to the platform; though no sooner had he attained this exalted position than two or three voices ordered him in a peremptory manner to "Come down!"
10027{_}1426{_}The greater part of the audience not caring the toss of a button whether Fletcher started his magazine or not, but thinking that it was rather good fun to interrupt the proceedings, now joined the opposition, and the unfortunate Bibbs was subjected to a brisk fire of chaff.
10027{_}1427{_}One facetious class-mate, standing close to the sink, offered to sell him by auction; and hammering on the stones with the fragment of a bat handle, knocked him down for threepence to another joker, who said he'd do for a pen-wiper.
10027{_}1428{_}"Sing a song, Bibbs!"
10027{_}1429{_}cried one voice; "Where's your neck-tie?"
10027{_}1430{_}asked another; "What are you grinning at?"
10027{_}1431{_}demanded a third; while the object of these pleasantries stood, with a vacant smile upon his face, nervously fumbling with his watch-chain.
10027{_}1432{_}"Go on!"
10027{_}1433{_}cried Fletcher, who had descended from the platform to make room for his colleague; "say something, you fool!"
10027{_}1434{_}"The magazine is to be written on exercise-book paper," began Bibbs, and had only got thus far when he was interrupted by a perfect salvo of paper bags which little "Rats" discharged in quick succession.
10027{_}1435{_}With an exclamation of wrath Fletcher made a dive in the direction of the offender, and in a moment the whole gathering was in a state of confusion.
10027{_}1436{_}The majority of those present siding with "Rats," began to hustle Fletcher, while two gentlemen having dragged Bibbs from his perch, jumped up in his stead, and began to execute a clog-dance.
10027{_}1437{_}In the midst of this commotion Maxton elbowed his way through the crush, and having pushed the two boys off the sink, mounted it himself, crying,--  "Look here, I'm going to speak; just you listen a minute.
10027{_}1438{_}The reason why Bibbs wants to start a new magazine is because he wrote a novel once, and sent it to _The Ronleian_ to come out so much each month, and they wouldn't have it."
10027{_}1439{_}"Shut up, Maxton!"
10027{_}1440{_}cried Fletcher, rushing to the spot; "you've only come here on purpose to interrupt.
10027{_}1441{_}Let's turn him out!"
10027{_}1442{_}"Yes, turn him out!"
10027{_}1443{_}echoed the audience, who by this time were just in the spirit for "ragging," and would have ejected friend or foe alike for the sport of the thing--"turn him out!"
10027{_}1444{_}The two clog-dancers being quite ready to avenge the interruption of their performance, formed themselves into a storming-party, and carried the platform by assault.
10027{_}1445{_}Maxton, struggling all the way, was dragged to the door, and cast out into the playground.
10027{_}1446{_}Most of the restless spirits in the audience requiring a short breathing-space to recover their wind after the tussle, there followed a few moments' quiet, which Fletcher immediately took advantage of to mount the sink and resume the business of the meeting.
10027{_}1447{_}"The magazine," he began, "is going to be written on exercise-book paper.
10027{_}1448{_}Any one who likes can contribute, and it's going to be more especially a paper for the Third Form."
10027{_}1449{_}The speaker went on to show that the periodical was destined to supply a long-felt want.
10027{_}1450{_}_The Ronleian_ ignored the doings of boys in the lower half of the school, and returned their contributions with insulting suggestions, pencilled on the margins, that the authors should devote some of their spare time and energy to the study of their English grammars and spelling-books.
10027{_}1451{_}_The Third Form Chronicle_, as it was to be called, would recognize the fact that junior boys had as much right to be heard as seniors, and would afford them the opportunity of airing their views on any subject they chose to bring forward.
10027{_}1452{_}Fletcher had barely time to proceed thus far with his speech when an alarming interruption occurred, which put an immediate stop to his further utterance.
10027{_}1453{_}Nearly at the top of the end wall there had formerly been a ventilator; this, for one reason or another, had been removed, and in the brickwork an open space about a foot square had been left.
10027{_}1454{_}A hissing noise was suddenly heard outside, and the next moment a stream of water shot through the aperture, and descended in a perfect deluge on the heads of the company.
10027{_}1455{_}The fact was that Maxton, ever a reckless young villain, had discovered a hose fixed to one of the mains close to the building, and had immediately seized upon it as an instrument wherewith to wreak vengeance on his companions for having turned him out of the meeting.
10027{_}1456{_}Words cannot describe the uproar and confusion which followed.
10027{_}1457{_}As one man the whole assembly made for the door, but only to find it fastened on the outside.
10027{_}1458{_}The water flew all over the small building, drenching every one in turn.
10027{_}1459{_}Some howled, some laughed, and only Bibbs had sufficient presence of mind to creep under the sink, which afforded a certain amount of shelter from the falling flood.
10027{_}1460{_}The deluge ceased as suddenly as it had begun, and an instant later the door was flung open, and the figure of a Sixth Form boy was seen barring the exit.
10027{_}1461{_}"Now, then," he demanded, "what are you youngsters making this awful row for?
10027{_}1462{_}I've a jolly good mind to take all your names."
10027{_}1463{_}There was a moment's silence.
10027{_}1464{_}Then Fletcher's voice was heard exclaiming,--  "Oh!
10027{_}1465{_}it's only old 'Thirsty;' he's all right."
10027{_}1466{_}"Here, not so fast," answered the prefect, blocking up the doorway as some boys tried to escape; "what are you chaps doing in here?
10027{_}1467{_}I thought you'd been told to keep out."
10027{_}1468{_}The originator of the meeting pushed his way through the crowd, and taking hold of the big fellow's arm in a familiar manner, said,--  "Oh, it's all right, 'Thirsty,' old chap.
10027{_}1469{_}We just came inside, and some one squirted water all over us, and that's why we shouted.
10027{_}1470{_}But we won't do it again."
10027{_}1471{_}"Oh, but it isn't all right," returned the other.
10027{_}1472{_}"If I find any of you in here again, I'll help you out with the toe of my boot.
10027{_}1473{_}Go on!
10027{_}1474{_}I'll let you off this once."
10027{_}1475{_}The crowd rushed forth and quickly dispersed.
10027{_}1476{_}"That Thurston seems an awful decent chap," said Diggory; "I didn't think he'd let us off so easily."
10027{_}1477{_}"He's all right as long as you don't cross him," answered Carton.
10027{_}1478{_}"He used to be pretty strict, but he doesn't seem to care now what fellows do.
10027{_}1479{_}He's very thick with Fletcher's brother--that's one reason why he didn't do anything just now; but I can tell you he's a nasty chap to deal with when he's in a wax."
10027{_}1480{_}The prefect locked the empty building, and turning on his heel caught sight of our three friends, who were standing close by waiting for "Rats."
10027{_}1481{_}"Hullo, you new kids!
10027{_}1482{_}what are you called?"
10027{_}1483{_}The usual answer was given, and Thurston passed on, little thinking what good cause he would have before the end of the year for remembering the names of the trio, and altogether unaware of the prominent part which the Triple Alliance was destined to play in his own private affairs as well as in the fortunes of Ronleigh College.
10027{_}1484{_}CHAPTER IX.
10027{_}1485{_}A HOLIDAY ADVENTURE.
10027{_}1486{_}The weeks slipped away, and the Triple Alliance soon got over their new-boy trials, and began to enjoy all the rights and privileges of Ronleigh College boys.
10027{_}1487{_}They wrote letters to Miss Eleanor and to their former schoolfellows, and received in reply the latest news from The Birches.
10027{_}1488{_}"The Philistines are quite friendly now," wrote Acton.
10027{_}1489{_}"We had a match against them last week on their ground, and they gave us tea after.
10027{_}1490{_}It's awfully slow; I almost wish that chap Noaks was back."
10027{_}1491{_}"So do I," added Diggory, as he finished the sentence; "we could very well spare him."
10027{_}1492{_}"Oh, he's all right," answered Jack Vance; "that row's blown over now.
10027{_}1493{_}As long as we leave him alone he won't interfere with us."
10027{_}1494{_}"Won't he!"
10027{_}1495{_}returned the other; "you take my word for it, he hasn't forgotten what you said about his father, and he's only waiting for a chance to pay us out.
10027{_}1496{_}Whenever I go near him he looks as black as ink."
10027{_}1497{_}It was customary at Ronleigh to have what was called a half-term holiday.
10027{_}1498{_}This was usually given on a Monday, to enable those boys who lived within a short distance of the school to spend the week end at home; while, in the winter or spring terms, the boarders who remained at the school usually devoted the greater portion of the day to a paper-chase.
10027{_}1499{_}"I shall go home," said Jack Vance to his two chums; "Todderton's only about half an hour's ride from here on the railway.
10027{_}1500{_}And, I say, I've got a grand idea: I'm going to write and get my mater to invite you fellows to come too!
10027{_}1501{_}It would be jolly to have a meeting there of the Triple Alliance, and I'm sure old Denson would let you go if we came back on Monday night."
10027{_}1502{_}Both Mugford and Diggory were charmed with the idea.
10027{_}1503{_}"But d'you really think your mater would have us?"
10027{_}1504{_}they asked.
10027{_}1505{_}"Of course she will, if I ask her," answered Jack, and straightway sat down to write the letter.
10027{_}1506{_}By Wednesday evening everything, including the formal invitation and the doctors permission to accept the same, had been obtained, and for the two following days the Triple Alliance could talk or think of little else besides their projected excursion.
10027{_}1507{_}At length Saturday came, and as soon as morning school was over they rushed upstairs to change into their best clothes; and having crammed their night-shirts, brushes and combs, etc., into a hand-bag, hurried off to the railway station, in order that they might, as Jack put it, "be home in time for dinner."
10027{_}1508{_}Just as they were getting into the train, who should come out of the booking-office but young Noaks.
10027{_}1509{_}"Hullo!"
10027{_}1510{_}said Jack.
10027{_}1511{_}"He must be going home too; I hope he won't come in here."
10027{_}1512{_}The new-comer, however, had no intention of making another attempt to force his society on the Triple Alliance; he passed them with a surly nod, and entered a compartment at the other end of the train.
10027{_}1513{_}Jack Vance lived in the suburbs of Todderton, about twenty minutes' walk from the railway; but for all that he managed to carry out his intention of being home in time for dinner; and the three boys, after receiving a hearty welcome, were soon seated down to a repast which came very acceptable after seven weeks of school fare.
10027{_}1514{_}"Jack," said Mr. Vance, "you know that house that was to let just on the other side of The Hermitage?
10027{_}1515{_}Who d'you think's taken it?"
10027{_}1516{_}"I don't know, father."
10027{_}1517{_}"Why, that man Simpson, the uncle of your friend what's-his-name."
10027{_}1518{_}"He isn't my friend," answered Jack.
10027{_}1519{_}"You mean Noaks.
10027{_}1520{_}Fancy his coming to live so near to us as that!
10027{_}1521{_}We saw him in the train just now.
10027{_}1522{_}He's here for the holiday."
10027{_}1523{_}"I ought to tell you," continued Mr. Vance, turning to Diggory, "that our next-door neighbour is called 'The Hermit.'
10027{_}1524{_}He's a queer old fellow, who lives by himself, and never makes friends or speaks to any one.
10027{_}1525{_}He's supposed to be very clever, and I've heard it said that he's got a very valuable collection of coins, and is quite an authority on the subject; it's one of his hobbies."
10027{_}1526{_}"I suppose," said Mugford thoughtfully, "that as he's a hermit that's why his place is called The Hermitage."
10027{_}1527{_}"Well done, Mug!"
10027{_}1528{_}said Jack, speaking with his mouth pretty full; "you're getting quite sharp."
10027{_}1529{_}"Yes, that's it," continued Mr. Vance, laughing.
10027{_}1530{_}"The old man's away from home just now; he was suffering from rheumatism very badly, and the doctor ordered him to a course of treatment at some baths."
10027{_}1531{_}The conversation turned on other topics, and when at length they rose from the table, Jack proposed a stroll round the garden.
10027{_}1532{_}There were many things to see--some pet rabbits, a swing, and an old summer-house, which Jack, being, we should say, of a decidedly nautical turn of mind, had turned into a sort of miniature shipbuilding yard for the construction of model vessels; though at present the chief use to which the place seemed to have been put was the production of a great amount of chips and shavings.
10027{_}1533{_}"I say," exclaimed the owner, after he and his friends had amused themselves for some time boring holes in the door with a brace, "I know what we'll do: let's go over and explore The Hermitage!"
10027{_}1534{_}Anything with a spice of excitement in it was meat and drink to Diggory.
10027{_}1535{_}He immediately seconded the proposition, and Mugford, after a moment's hesitation, agreed to join his companions in the enterprise.
10027{_}1536{_}They strolled off down the path, and soon reached a long stretch of brick wall, the top of which was thickly covered with fragments of broken bottles.
10027{_}1537{_}"There's a place down at the other end where we can get over," said Jack.
10027{_}1538{_}"I smashed the glass with a hammer, because I lost a ball and had to climb over and get it, one day last holidays."
10027{_}1539{_}The Hermitage was surrounded on all sides by a thick mass of shrubs and trees, through which a moment later the Triple Alliance were cautiously threading their way.
10027{_}1540{_}Emerging from the bushes, they found themselves standing on a gravel path, green with moss and weeds, which ran round the house--a queer, dilapidated-looking building, which seemed sadly in want of repair: the plaster was cracked and discoloured, while the doors and windows had long stood in need of a fresh coating of paint.
10027{_}1541{_}"I say," whispered Mugford, "hadn't we better go back?
10027{_}1542{_}what if the old chap's at home!"
10027{_}1543{_}"Oh, it's all right; there's nobody about," answered Jack.
10027{_}1544{_}"Let's go on and see what the place is really like."
10027{_}1545{_}They tip-toed round the building.
10027{_}1546{_}It was evidently unoccupied, though the delightful sense of uncertainty that at any moment some one might pounce out upon them or walk down the drive made the questionable adventure very charming.
10027{_}1547{_}"Have you ever been inside?"
10027{_}1548{_}asked Diggory.
10027{_}1549{_}"No, rather not; I don't think any one has except the doctor, and an old woman who comes in to do the house-work."
10027{_}1550{_}"Well, then, I'm going in," answered Diggory, with a twinkle in his eye.
10027{_}1551{_}"Go on!
10027{_}1552{_}Why, you might be had up for house-breaking!"
10027{_}1553{_}"Rubbish!
10027{_}1554{_}I'm not going to steal anything.--Here, Mug, lend me your knife a minute."
10027{_}1555{_}"I don't believe this one's fastened," he continued, walking up to one of the windows.
10027{_}1556{_}"No, it isn't.
10027{_}1557{_}Bother!
10027{_}1558{_}I'm awfully sorry, Mugford."
10027{_}1559{_}Using the big blade of the clasp-knife as a lever, Diggory had just succeeded in raising the sash the fraction of an inch, when the steel suddenly snapped off short at the handle.
10027{_}1560{_}"Oh, never mind," said the owner; "let's go back now.
10027{_}1561{_}What if we're seen!"
10027{_}1562{_}"Oh, there's no fear of that," answered Jack, who was always infected with the adventurous spirit of his chum.--"Go on, Diggy; I'll come too."
10027{_}1563{_}By inserting their fingers in the aperture, the boys soon raised the sash, and a few seconds later Diggory mounted the ledge and scrambled through the window "Come on," he said; "the coast's all clear."
10027{_}1564{_}Jack Vance joined him immediately, and Mugford, not wishing to be left alone outside, was not long in making up his mind to follow his companions.
10027{_}1565{_}The room in which the three boys found themselves was evidently a library or study.
10027{_}1566{_}Book-shelves, and cupboards with glass doors, containing geological and other specimens, occupied much of the wall space; while in the centre of the floor stood a large writing-table, covered with a miscellaneous collection of pens, ink-pots, bundles of papers, and a polished mahogany box which could easily be recognized as a microscope-case.
10027{_}1567{_}The intruders stood for a few moments gazing round in silence.
10027{_}1568{_}The place did not look very interesting, and smelt rather damp and mouldy.
10027{_}1569{_}"I say," exclaimed Jack Vance, "look there: he don't seem very careful how he leaves his things when he goes away."
10027{_}1570{_}As he spoke he pointed across to the opposite side of the room, where, between two bookcases, an iron safe had been let into the wall.
10027{_}1571{_}The heavy door was standing half open, while the floor beneath was strewn with a quantity of shallow wooden trays lined with green baize.
10027{_}1572{_}"Old bachelors are always untidy," remarked Diggory.
10027{_}1573{_}"Let's see where this door leads to."
10027{_}1574{_}He turned the handle as he spoke, and walked out into a gloomy little hall paved with cold, bare flagstones, which caused their footsteps to waken mournful echoes in the empty house.
10027{_}1575{_}"I say, you fellows, don't let's go any further," murmured Mugford;" we've seen enough now.
10027{_}1576{_}Suppose the old chap came back and--"  He never reached the end of the sentence, for Diggory suddenly raised his hand, exclaiming in a whisper, "Hark!
10027{_}1577{_}what was that?"
10027{_}1578{_}The loud ticking of Mugford's old turnip of a watch was distinctly audible in the silence which followed.
10027{_}1579{_}"What is it, Diggy?
10027{_}1580{_}what--"  "Hark!
10027{_}1581{_}there it is again; listen."
10027{_}1582{_}The suspense became awful.
10027{_}1583{_}At length Diggory dropped his hand.
10027{_}1584{_}"Didn't you hear footsteps?"
10027{_}1585{_}he asked.
10027{_}1586{_}"I'm certain there's some one walking about on the gravel path."
10027{_}1587{_}"We shall be caught," whimpered Mugford; "I knew we should.
10027{_}1588{_}What can we do?"
10027{_}1589{_}"Bolt!"
10027{_}1590{_}answered Diggory, and began tip-toeing back towards the library door.
10027{_}1591{_}"Stay here half a 'jiffy,'" he added; "I'll go and reconnoitre."
10027{_}1592{_}Ages seemed to pass while Jack Vance and Mugford stood in the dark passage awaiting their companion's return.
10027{_}1593{_}At length the door was pushed softly open.
10027{_}1594{_}"It's all right; there's no one there.
10027{_}1595{_}I must have been mistaken.
10027{_}1596{_}Come along."
10027{_}1597{_}In a very short time the Triple Alliance were once more outside The Hermitage.
10027{_}1598{_}Diggory lingered for a moment to close the window, and then followed his companions through the shrubs and over the wall.
10027{_}1599{_}"You are a great ass, Diggy, to go giving us a start like that," said Jack, as they paused for a moment to take breath before returning to the house.
10027{_}1600{_}"Well, I could have sworn I heard the gravel crunch as if some one was walking on it," returned the other.
10027{_}1601{_}"I should think the place must be haunted."
10027{_}1602{_}A good tea, with all kinds of nice things on the table, soon revived the boys from the trifling shock which their nerves had sustained, and by the end of the evening their adventure was wellnigh forgotten.
10027{_}1603{_}They were destined, however, to remember it for many a long day to come, and before many hours had passed they were heartily wishing that they had never set foot inside The Hermitage, but kept on their own side of the wall.
10027{_}1604{_}The party were seated at supper on Sunday evening, when a servant entered the room, and addressing her master said, "If you please, sir, there's a policeman called to see you."
10027{_}1605{_}Jack's father rose from his chair, remarking, in a jocular manner, "I expect it's one of you young gentlemen he's come after."
10027{_}1606{_}The meal was nearly over when Mr. Vance returned and reseated himself at the table.
10027{_}1607{_}"Did either of you hear the dog bark last night?"
10027{_}1608{_}he asked.
10027{_}1609{_}"No; why?"
10027{_}1610{_}"Why, because old Fossberry's house has been broken into, and they think the thieves must have come through our garden; there were some footmarks in the shrubbery just on the other side of the wall."
10027{_}1611{_}The hearts of the Triple Alliance seemed to jump into their throats, and their mouths grew dry and parched.
10027{_}1612{_}Jack stared at Mugford, and Mugford stared at Diggory, but none of them spoke.
10027{_}1613{_}"It seems," continued Mr. Vance, not noticing the effect which his first announcement had produced on at least three of his hearers, "that the old woman who looks after the house went there this morning, and found that the iron safe in which the old chap keeps his coins had been opened and the whole collection removed.
10027{_}1614{_}The only trace of the thieves that the police have been able to discover is the broken blade of a clasp-knife, which was on a flower-bed near the window."
10027{_}1615{_}"What will they get if they are caught?"
10027{_}1616{_}asked Jack faintly.
10027{_}1617{_}"Oh, penal servitude, I suppose; it's a serious business housebreaking."
10027{_}1618{_}"How quiet you boys are!"
10027{_}1619{_}said Mrs. Vance a short time later.
10027{_}1620{_}"I think you must be tired.
10027{_}1621{_}Wouldn't you like to go to bed?"
10027{_}1622{_}The three friends were only too glad to avail themselves of this excuse for getting away into some place where they could indulge in a little private conversation.
10027{_}1623{_}Diggory and Mugford slept together in the same room; Jack followed them in and closed the door.
10027{_}1624{_}"Well," he exclaimed, "we're in a nice mess."
10027{_}1625{_}"But we didn't steal the coins," said Mugford.
10027{_}1626{_}"Of course we didn't--the safe had been robbed before we went there--but it looks as if we'd done it; and if they find out we got into the house, I don't see how we're going to prove that we're innocent."
10027{_}1627{_}There was a short silence; then Diggory spoke.
10027{_}1628{_}"Look here, Jack: I was the one who proposed going inside the place; shall I tell your guv'nor?"
10027{_}1629{_}"Well, I was thinking of doing that myself, only I don't see what good it can do.
10027{_}1630{_}If we tell him, he'll be bound to tell the police, to explain about those footmarks; and when it comes out that we got into the house, I should think we are pretty certain to be charged with having stolen the coins.
10027{_}1631{_}I think the best thing will be to keep it dark: we didn't crib the things, and the thieves are sure to be caught in time."
10027{_}1632{_}Even after Jack had retired to his own room, Diggory and Mugford lay awake for hours discussing the situation; and when at length they did fall asleep, it was only to dream of being chased by "The Hermit" and a swarm of long-legged policemen, who forced their way into the Third Form classroom at Ronleigh, and handcuffed the unfortunate trio in the very bosom of "The Happy Family."
10027{_}1633{_}The following morning was spent in visiting such parts of the town of Todderton as were worth seeing.
10027{_}1634{_}"Upon my word," said Jack, "I feel funky to show my nose outside our gate, just as if I really had prigged those wretched coins.
10027{_}1635{_}I shan't be at all sorry this evening to get back to Ronleigh.
10027{_}1636{_}It's all in the paper this morning; it mentions the footmarks and the knife-blade, and says that as yet the police have not been able to discover any further traces of the robbers."
10027{_}1637{_}The conditions on which the half-term holiday was granted required every boy to return to school on the Monday evening, and accordingly, about seven o'clock, the Triple Alliance found themselves once more on their way to the railway station.
10027{_}1638{_}They took their seats, and had hardly done so when young Noaks entered the compartment.
10027{_}1639{_}"Hullo, you fellows!"
10027{_}1640{_}he exclaimed; "didn't you hear me whistle?
10027{_}1641{_}I was standing over there by the book-stall."
10027{_}1642{_}Regarding this as an overture of friendship after their recent encounter, Jack Vance replied in an equally amicable manner, and after a few common-place remarks the party relapsed into silence.
10027{_}1643{_}At Chatton, the station before Ronleigh, a man who had so far travelled with them got out, and the four boys were left alone.
10027{_}1644{_}Hardly had the train started again when Noaks put down his paper, and turning to his companions said,--  "That's a rum business about that old chap's house being robbed, isn't it?"
10027{_}1645{_}Something in the speaker's look and in the tone of his voice caused the three listeners to experience an unpleasant quickening of their pulses.
10027{_}1646{_}"Yes," answered Diggory, with a well-assumed air of indifference.
10027{_}1647{_}"I suppose they'll catch the thieves in time."
10027{_}1648{_}"I suppose so," returned the other, "especially if they find the chap who owns that knife with the broken blade."
10027{_}1649{_}The malignant look with which these words was accompanied showed at once that the speaker meant mischief.
10027{_}1650{_}The three friends looked at one another in horrified amazement.
10027{_}1651{_}Could it be possible that their visit to The Hermitage had already been discovered?
10027{_}1652{_}Noaks watched their faces for a moment, evidently well pleased with the effect which his remark had produced; then he burst out laughing.
10027{_}1653{_}"Look here," he continued, producing from his pocket a buck-handled clasp-knife: "I wonder if that's anything like it; I see the big blade's broken."
10027{_}1654{_}The Triple Alliance recognized it in a moment as one of the articles that had been rescued from Mugford's sale at The Birches; in fact, the owner's name appeared plainly engraved on the small brass plate.
10027{_}1655{_}Diggory was the first to find his tongue.
10027{_}1656{_}"What d'you mean?
10027{_}1657{_}We didn't steal the coins!"
10027{_}1658{_}"My dear fellow, I never said you did.
10027{_}1659{_}I only know that on Saturday I was looking over our wall, through an opening there happens to be in the shrubs, and saw you fellows climbing out of the old chap's window; and after you'd gone I noticed something lying in the path, and I hopped over, and picked up this knife."
10027{_}1660{_}"Give it here; it's mine," said Mugford, holding out his hand.
10027{_}1661{_}"No fear," answered the other, calmly returning the piece of lost property to his own pocket.
10027{_}1662{_}"In this case finding's keeping; besides, I'm not sure if I couldn't get a reward for this if I sent it to the right place."
10027{_}1663{_}The train began to slacken speed as it approached Ronleigh station.
10027{_}1664{_}"Look here, Noaks," cried Jack Vance, in a fit of desperation, "what are you going to do?
10027{_}1665{_}You know very well we are not thieves."
10027{_}1666{_}"I don't know anything of the sort," returned the tormentor, standing up to take his bag off the rack; "all I know is just what I've told you.
10027{_}1667{_}See here, Mr. Vance," he continued, rounding on Jack with a sudden snarl, "you were good enough some little time ago to make some very caddish remarks about my father; in the future you'd better keep your mouth shut.
10027{_}1668{_}I owe all three of you a dressing down for things that happened at Chatford, and now you'd better mind your P's and Q's if you don't want to be hauled up for housebreaking."
10027{_}1669{_}With this parting threat the ex-Philistine left the carriage.
10027{_}1670{_}Mugford, Jack, and Diggory gazed at one another for a moment with anything but a happy look on their faces.
10027{_}1671{_}One after another they slowly gathered up their things and stepped out on to the platform.
10027{_}1672{_}Hardly had they done so when they heard their names called, and turning round beheld the small figure of "Rats" rushing forward to meet them.
10027{_}1673{_}"Hullo!"
10027{_}1674{_}he exclaimed.
10027{_}1675{_}"Old Ally sent me down to get a paper, and I thought you'd come by this train.
10027{_}1676{_}I say, there's a fine row on up at the school--such a lark; I'll tell you about it as we go along."
10027{_}1677{_}CHAPTER X.
10027{_}1678{_}A SCREW LOOSE IN THE SIXTH.
10027{_}1679{_}For the time being the three friends forgot their own troubles in their eagerness to hear "Rat's" description of certain events which had happened during their absence from Ronleigh.
10027{_}1680{_}"Look sharp; out with it!"
10027{_}1681{_}they exclaimed.
10027{_}1682{_}"What's happened?"
10027{_}1683{_}"Well," began Rathson, "it all came out through young Bayley acting the fool and spraining his ankle.
10027{_}1684{_}You know we had the paper-chase this morning, and the hares ran out to Arrow Hill, and back again round by the canal and Birksam Church.
10027{_}1685{_}Just after we'd rounded the hill, young Bayley jumped off the top of a high hedge, and twisted his foot so badly that he couldn't stand up.
10027{_}1686{_}As it happened, there was a check just then, and Carton ran forward and told Allingford what had happened.
10027{_}1687{_}He and Oaks came back, and said the only thing would be to get him to Chatton station, and so home by train.
10027{_}1688{_}It was awfully decent of those chaps.
10027{_}1689{_}They carried Bayley all the way, and then Oaks went home with him, and Allingford walked back, and so, of course, they missed half the run.
10027{_}1690{_}Awfully brickish of them I call it, considering that it was only a kid like Bayley."
10027{_}1691{_}The Triple Alliance gave a murmur of assent.
10027{_}1692{_}"Was that what the row's about?"
10027{_}1693{_}asked Diggory.
10027{_}1694{_}"Oh, bless you, no; I haven't come to that yet.
10027{_}1695{_}After he'd seen Oaks and Bayley into the train, old Ally started to walk home.
10027{_}1696{_}There's a little 'pub' about half a mile out of Chatton called the Black Swan, and he thought he'd call and ask if they'd seen the fellows pass.
10027{_}1697{_}You know Thurston the prefect, that chap who came to the door when we were having that meeting in the 'old lab.'
10027{_}1698{_}Well, now, if he and Mouler, and two or three more of that sort, weren't sitting in the taproom, smoking, and drinking beer, and having a regular high old time.
10027{_}1699{_}They'd lagged behind on purpose.
10027{_}1700{_}Of course Allingford kicked them all out, and he and 'Thirsty' had a frightful row.
10027{_}1701{_}They say the big chaps want to hush the matter up as far as they can, and not report it to old Denson, for fear he'd make it an excuse to put a stop to paper-chasing.
10027{_}1702{_}Ally slanged Thurston right and left, and told him that if he chose to drink beer in a low 'pub' with the biggest blackguards in the school, he needn't expect that the fellows in the Sixth would have anything to do with him, and that he ought to send in his resignation as a prefect."
10027{_}1703{_}On entering the school buildings, our three friends were convinced of the truth of their comrade's story, and on their way to the schoolroom the question was repeated at least half a dozen times--"Have you heard about old 'Thirsty' being cobbed in the Black Swan?"
10027{_}1704{_}Diggory thought of the conversation he had overheard in Acton's study, and mentioned it to Carton.
10027{_}1705{_}"Yes," answered the latter.
10027{_}1706{_}"Big Fletcher's a beast.
10027{_}1707{_}I know Thurston's very chummy with him, but I don't see that's got much to do with it.
10027{_}1708{_}My brother, who left last term, said that 'Thirsty' used to be rather a jolly chap, only he's got a fearful temper when he's crossed.
10027{_}1709{_}Most of the chaps like him as a prefect, because as long as you don't interfere with him he doesn't seem to care much what any one does.
10027{_}1710{_}The real thing is he's going to the dogs, and, as Allingford says, he ought to resign."
10027{_}1711{_}Away in one of the Sixth Form studies the subject of their conversation was sitting with his hands in his pockets, frowning at the fire.
10027{_}1712{_}He was roused from his reverie by some one putting his head round the corner of the door and exclaiming,--  "Hullo, 'Thirsty!'"
10027{_}1713{_}"Hullo, Fletcher!
10027{_}1714{_}where on earth have you been all the evening?"
10027{_}1715{_}The new-comer was tall and lanky; he had a sharp, foxy-looking face, with thin, straight lips, and two deep lines which looked almost like scars between the eyebrows.
10027{_}1716{_}He shut the door, and dragging forward a chair, sat down with his feet on the fender, and commenced warming his hands at the fire.
10027{_}1717{_}"Oh, I've been nowhere in particular," he answered, laughing.
10027{_}1718{_}"But I say, young man, you seem to have raised a pretty good hornets' nest about your ears along this corridor."
10027{_}1719{_}"Yes, I know; they've had the cheek to send me that!"
10027{_}1720{_}He leaned back as he spoke, and taking a piece of paper from the table, tossed it across to his friend.
10027{_}1721{_}It was a letter signed by most of the prefects, suggesting that he should send in his resignation.
10027{_}1722{_}"Humph!"
10027{_}1723{_}said Fletcher; "that's a nice sort of a round robin, don't you call it?
10027{_}1724{_}Well, what are you going to do?"
10027{_}1725{_}"Oh, I shall resign and have done with it.
10027{_}1726{_}I'm sick of having to masquerade about as a good boy.
10027{_}1727{_}I mean to do what I like."
10027{_}1728{_}"Pooh!"
10027{_}1729{_}returned the other.
10027{_}1730{_}"Now that you are a prefect, I wouldn't give up all the privileges and the right to go out and come in when you like just because a strait-laced chap like Allingford chooses to take offence at something you do.
10027{_}1731{_}They can't force you to resign unless they go to the doctor, and they won't do that.
10027{_}1732{_}I know what I'd do: I'd tell them pretty straight to go and be hanged, and keep their sermonizing to themselves."
10027{_}1733{_}Thurston turned on the speaker with a sudden burst of anger.
10027{_}1734{_}"Oh yes!"
10027{_}1735{_}he exclaimed; "you're always saying you'd do this and do that, but when the time comes you turn tail and sneak away.
10027{_}1736{_}Look here: you were the one who proposed going into the Black Swan this morning, and when young Mouler said Allingford was coming, you slipped out of the back door and left us to face the shindy."
10027{_}1737{_}"Well," returned the other, laughing, "I thought you chaps were going to bolt too.
10027{_}1738{_}I hopped over the wall at the back into the field, and waited there for about a quarter of an hour, and then, as no one came, I made tracks home."
10027{_}1739{_}"That's all very fine.
10027{_}1740{_}You took precious good care to save your own bacon; you always do."
10027{_}1741{_}"Oh, go on!"
10027{_}1742{_}answered Fletcher, rising from his chair; "you're in a wax to-night.
10027{_}1743{_}Well, ta, ta!
10027{_}1744{_}Don't you resign."
10027{_}1745{_}This little passage of arms was not the first of the kind that had taken place between Fletcher and Thurston, and it did not prevent a renewal of their friendship on the morrow.
10027{_}1746{_}The latter, following either his own inclination or the advice of his chum, decided not to resign his position as a prefect, and in a few days' time the majority of the school had wellnigh forgotten the fracas at the Black Swan.
10027{_}1747{_}Among those in high places, however, the affair was not so easily overlooked.
10027{_}1748{_}The big fellows kept their own counsel, but it soon became evident that Thurston was being "cut" and cold-shouldered by the other members of the Sixth; while he, for his part, as though by way of retaliation, began to hob-nob more freely than ever with boys lower down in the school and of decidedly questionable character.
10027{_}1749{_}"It's awfully bad form of a chap who's a prefect chumming up with a fellow like Mouler in the Upper Fourth," said Carton one afternoon.
10027{_}1750{_}"I wonder old 'Thirsty' isn't ashamed to do it.
10027{_}1751{_}And now he's hand and glove with those chaps Hawley and Gull in the Fifth; they've both got heaps of money, but they're frightful cads."
10027{_}1752{_}From the morning following their return to Ronleigh the Triple Alliance had been kept in a continual state of uneasiness and suspense, wondering what action Noaks would take regarding his discovery of their visit to The Hermitage.
10027{_}1753{_}The days passed by, and still he made no further reference to the matter, and took no notice of any of the three friends when he happened to pass them in the passages.
10027{_}1754{_}The fact was that for the time being his attention was turned in another direction.
10027{_}1755{_}Like most fellows of his kind, Noaks was a regular toady, ready to do anything in return for the privilege of being able to rub shoulders occasionally with some one in a higher position than himself, and he eagerly seized the opportunity which his friendship with Mouler afforded him of becoming intimate with Thurston.
10027{_}1756{_}It was rather a fine thing for a boy in the Upper Fourth to be accosted in a familiar manner by a prefect, and asked sometimes to visit the latter in his study; and when such things were possible, it was hardly worth while to spend time and attention in carrying on a feud with youngsters in the Third Form.
10027{_}1757{_}But Noaks had never forgotten the double humiliation he had suffered at Chatford--first in being sent off the football field, and again in the disastrous ending to the attempted raid on the Birchites' fireworks; nor had he forgiven the Triple Alliance for the part which they had played, especially on the latter occasion, in bringing shame and confusion on the heads of the Philistines.
10027{_}1758{_}One morning, nearly a month after the half-term holiday, the three friends were strolling arm in arm through the archway leading from the quadrangle to the paved playground, when they came face to face with their old enemy.
10027{_}1759{_}He was about to push past them without speaking; then, seeming suddenly to change his mind, he pulled up, took something from his pocket, and handing it to Jack Vance, said shortly,--  "There!
10027{_}1760{_}I thought you'd like to see that; it seems a good chance to earn some pocket-money."
10027{_}1761{_}The packet turned out to be a copy of the Todderton weekly paper.
10027{_}1762{_}"I've marked the place," added Noaks, turning on his heel with a sneering laugh; "you needn't give it me back."
10027{_}1763{_}A cross of blue chalk had been placed against a short paragraph appearing under the heading "Local Notes."
10027{_}1764{_}Jack read it out loud for the edification of his two companions.
10027{_}1765{_}"We notice that Mr. Fossberry has offered a reward of 50 pounds for any information which shall lead to the arrest of the thieves who entered his house some few weeks ago, and stole a valuable collection of coins.
10027{_}1766{_}As yet the police have been unable to discover any further traces of the missing property, but it is to be hoped that before long the offenders will be discovered and brought to justice."
10027{_}1767{_}There was a moment's silence.
10027{_}1768{_}"I wish I'd told my guv'nor," muttered Jack Vance.
10027{_}1769{_}"Well, tell him now," said Diggory.
10027{_}1770{_}"Oh no, I can't now; he'd wonder why I hadn't done it sooner.
10027{_}1771{_}Besides, I believe Noaks is only doing this to frighten us; he can't prove that we stole the coins, because we didn't.
10027{_}1772{_}All the same, it would be very awkward if he sent the police that jack-knife, and told them he'd seen us climbing out of the old chap's window."
10027{_}1773{_}"Yes," answered Diggory; "I suppose it would look rather fishy.
10027{_}1774{_}Bother him!
10027{_}1775{_}why can't he leave us alone?"
10027{_}1776{_}CHAPTER XI.
10027{_}1777{_}SHADOWS OF COMING EVENTS.
10027{_}1778{_}The Easter holidays came and went as rapidly as Easter  holidays always do, and before the Alliance had recovered from the excitement connected with their first experience of breaking up at Ronleigh, they were back again, greeting their friends, asking new boys their names, and, in short, commencing their second term as regular old stagers.
10027{_}1779{_}Up to the present they had been content to "lie low," and had remained satisfied with making the acquaintance of their class-mates in "The Happy Family;" but now they began to take more interest in school matters in general, and to notice what was going on in other circles besides their own.
10027{_}1780{_}In answer to the eager inquiries of his two companions, Jack Vance said that he had seen nothing of Noaks during the holidays, except having passed him on one or two occasions in the street.
10027{_}1781{_}The notice of the fifty pounds reward still appeared in the windows of the police station; but the robbery itself was beginning to be looked upon as a thing of the past, and was already wellnigh forgotten.
10027{_}1782{_}"I wonder if Noaks has still got my knife?"
10027{_}1783{_}said Mugford.
10027{_}1784{_}"Oh, I don't know," answered Jack.
10027{_}1785{_}"He's too much taken up with Mouler and Gull and all that lot to think about us.
10027{_}1786{_}I shouldn't bother my head about it any further; he only showed us that paper out of spite, to put us in a funk."
10027{_}1787{_}It was pretty evident, to the most casual observer, that the quarrel which the Black Swan incident had occasioned between Thurston and his brother prefects had not yet been dismissed from the minds of either party.
10027{_}1788{_}The former became more lax than ever in the discharge of his duties, and avoiding the society of his school equals, sought the companionship of such boys as Hawley, Gull, and Mouler, who at length came to be known throughout the College as "Thirsty's Lot."
10027{_}1789{_}With the exception of Fletcher, the prefects left him severely alone.
10027{_}1790{_}Allingford occasionally came down on him for allowing all kinds of misconduct to pass unchecked, but it was hardly to be expected that a fellow who was hand and glove with some of the principal offenders should have much influence or power in maintaining law and order; and these interviews with the captain usually ended in an exchange of black looks and angry words.
10027{_}1791{_}The consequences which resulted from this lack of harmony among those in authority may be easily imagined.
10027{_}1792{_}"Old Thirsty never makes a row when he sees a chap doing so-and-so," was the cry.
10027{_}1793{_}"Why should Oaks and Rowlands and those other fellows kick up bothers, and give lines for the same thing?"
10027{_}1794{_}To all these murmurers the prefects turned a deaf ear.
10027{_}1795{_}"I don't care what Thurston does," would be their answer; "you know the rule, and that's sufficient."
10027{_}1796{_}Any further remonstrance on the part of the offender was met with a summary "Shut up, or you'll get your head punched," and so for a time the matter ended.
10027{_}1797{_}It was hardly to be expected that the light-hearted juveniles of the Third Form should trouble their heads to take much notice of this disagreement among the seniors.
10027{_}1798{_}For one thing, they knew nothing of what was said and done in the Sixth Form studies, and even the prefects themselves never thought for a moment that this little bit of friction in the machinery of Ronleigh College would, figuratively speaking, lead to "hot bearings" and a narrow shave of a general breakdown.
10027{_}1799{_}So the members of "The Happy Family" pursued the even tenor of their way, getting into scrapes and scrambling out of them, feasting on pastry and ginger-beer, turning up in force on Saturday afternoon to witness the cricket matches, and coming to the conclusion that though Oaks and Rowlands might be a trifle strict, and rather freehanded with lines and "impots," yet all this could be overlooked and forgiven for the sake of the punishment which they inflicted on the enemy's bowling.
10027{_}1800{_}As it has been all along the intention of this story to follow the fortunes of the Triple Alliance, the record of their second term at Ronleigh would not be complete without some mention of their memorable adventure with the "coffee-mill."
10027{_}1801{_}Wednesday, the fourteenth of June, was Jack Vance's birthday, and just before morning school he expressed his intention of keeping it up in a novel manner.
10027{_}1802{_}"Look here!"
10027{_}1803{_}he remarked to his two companions.
10027{_}1804{_}"You know that little bootmaker's shop just down the road, before you come to the church.
10027{_}1805{_}There's a notice in the window, 'Double Tricycle on Hire.'
10027{_}1806{_}Well, the mater's sent me some money this year instead of a hamper, so I thought I'd hire the machine; and we'll go out for a ride, and take it in turns for one to walk or trot behind."
10027{_}1807{_}"Oh, I'd advise you not to!"
10027{_}1808{_}cried "Rats," who was standing by and overheard the project.
10027{_}1809{_}"Why not?"
10027{_}1810{_}"Why, it's a rotten old _sociable_, one of the first, I should think, that was ever made.
10027{_}1811{_}It's like working a tread-mill, and it rattles and bangs about until you think every minute it must all be coming to pieces.
10027{_}1812{_}It's got a sort of box-seat instead of a saddle.
10027{_}1813{_}Maxton hired it out one day the term before last, and he and I and Collis rode to Chatton.
10027{_}1814{_}It isn't meant to carry three; but the seat's very wide, and they squeezed me in between them.
10027{_}1815{_}There's something wrong with the steering-gear, and it makes a beastly grinding noise as it goes along, so Maxton christened it the 'coffee-mill.'
10027{_}1816{_}Fellows are always chaffing old Jobling about it, when they go into his shop to buy bits of leather, and asking him how much he'll take for his coffee-mill, and the old chap gets into an awful wax."
10027{_}1817{_}"Oh, I don't care!"
10027{_}1818{_}answered Jack.
10027{_}1819{_}"It'll be a lark, and we needn't go far.--What d'you say, Diggy?"
10027{_}1820{_}Diggory and Mugford both expressed their willingness to join in the expedition, and arrangements were accordingly made for it to take place that afternoon.
10027{_}1821{_}"You'd better not let old Jobling see three of you get on at once," said "Rats."
10027{_}1822{_}"I should send Mugford on in front and pick him up when you get round the corner."
10027{_}1823{_}Rathson's description of the "coffee-mill" was certainly not exaggerated.
10027{_}1824{_}It was a rusty, rattle-bag concern--a relic of the dark ages of cycling--and .looked as if it had not been used for a twelvemonth.
10027{_}1825{_}Jobling squirted some oil into the bearings, knocked the dust off the cushioned seat, and remarked that a shilling an hour was the proper charge; but that, as he always favoured the Ronleigh gentlemen, he would say two shillings, and they might keep it the whole afternoon.
10027{_}1826{_}Jack, as we have said before, was of rather a nautical turn of mind, and occasionally, when the fit was on him, loved to interlard his conversation with seafaring expressions.
10027{_}1827{_}"She isn't much of a craft to look at," he remarked, as they drew up and dismounted at the spot where Mugford stood waiting for them; "but we'll imagine this is my steam-yacht, and that we're going for a cruise.
10027{_}1828{_}Now then, Diggy, you're the mate, and you shall sit on the starboard side and steer.
10027{_}1829{_}Mugford's the passenger, so he'll go in the middle.
10027{_}1830{_}I'm captain, and I'll work the port treadles.
10027{_}1831{_}Now, then, all aboard!"
10027{_}1832{_}The boys scrambled on to the seat, and with some little amount of crushing and squeezing got settled in their places, and at the captain's word, "Half-speed ahead!"
10027{_}1833{_}the voyage commenced.
10027{_}1834{_}They went lumbering and clattering through the outskirts of the town, and at length, after having roused the dormant wit of one shop-boy, who shouted "Knives to grind!"
10027{_}1835{_}after them, they gained the highroad.
10027{_}1836{_}For half a mile the voyage was prosperous enough; then the adventures began.
10027{_}1837{_}They were going at a good pace down a gentle slope, and on turning a corner saw immediately in front of them a narrow piece of road with a duck-pond on one side and a high bank on the other.
10027{_}1838{_}Some one had carelessly left a wheelbarrow standing very nearly in the centre of the highway, and there was only just room to pass it on the water side.
10027{_}1839{_}"Starboard a little!"
10027{_}1840{_}The steering gear worked rather stiffly.
10027{_}1841{_}Diggory gave the handle a hard twist, and it went round further than he intended.
10027{_}1842{_}"Port!"
10027{_}1843{_}cried the captain, "hard a-port!"
10027{_}1844{_}But it was too late, and the next moment the "coffee-mill" ran down the sloping bank and plunged into the duck-pond.
10027{_}1845{_}It gave a violent lurch, but fortunately its breadth of beam kept it from overturning, and the water, being not more than a few inches deep, only wet the boots of the mariners.
10027{_}1846{_}"You great ass, Diggy!
10027{_}1847{_}why didn't you _port?_" demanded the captain.
10027{_}1848{_}The mate, who as a matter of fact could not have told the difference between the nautical "port" and home-made ginger-beer, answered promptly, "So I did;" and the two officers commenced to punch each other with their disengaged hands.
10027{_}1849{_}This combat, which was conducted with the utmost good feeling on both sides, had been continued for nearly a minute, when the passenger, on whose unoffending back a large proportion of the blows were falling, remarked,--  "Well, if we aren't going to stop here all day, when you've quite done we'd better think about getting out."
10027{_}1850{_}They were at least four yards from the shore, and it was impossible to reach it dry-shod.
10027{_}1851{_}"Some one must take off his boots and socks and haul her out," said Diggory.
10027{_}1852{_}"Well, I can't," answered Jack; "the captain never ought to leave the ship."
10027{_}1853{_}"Oh, I'll go," answered Mugford, laughing; and accordingly, after performing some complicated gymnastic feats in getting off his boots, he slid from the seat into the water, and so hauled the "coffee-mill" back to _terra firma_.
10027{_}1854{_}It would be impossible to describe in detail all the alarming incidents which happened during the outward passage.
10027{_}1855{_}They had not gone a quarter of a mile further when something went wrong with the brake.
10027{_}1856{_}They flew down a long hill, holding on for dear life, nothing but the grand way in which the mate managed this time to steer a straight course down the middle of the road saving them from destruction.
10027{_}1857{_}Nevertheless, mounting the last slope was such hard labour that Mugford had to turn to and "work his passage," by every now and again taking a spell at the treadles.
10027{_}1858{_}"Look here!"
10027{_}1859{_}said Diggory at length: "don't you think we've gone far enough?
10027{_}1860{_}we shan't be back in time for tea."
10027{_}1861{_}"Oh, I forgot," answered the captain.
10027{_}1862{_}"We'll see.
10027{_}1863{_}Stand by your anchor!
10027{_}1864{_}Let go-o-o!"
10027{_}1865{_}The "coffee-mill" stopped, and Jack Vance pulled out his watch.
10027{_}1866{_}"By me it's half-past twelve, and I'm four hours slow: twelve to one, one to two, two to three, three to four--half-past four.
10027{_}1867{_}Yes, it's time we turned round.
10027{_}1868{_}Now, then, 'bout ship!"
10027{_}1869{_}The tricycle clanked and rattled away merrily enough on the return journey until it came to the long hill, which this time had to be climbed instead of descended.
10027{_}1870{_}"Don't let's get off," said Jack; "we ought to rush her up this if we set our minds to it."
10027{_}1871{_}With a great deal of panting and struggling they succeeded in getting about half-way; then suddenly there was a crack, and the machine, instead of going forward, began to run back.
10027{_}1872{_}Faster and faster it went, the pedals remaining motionless under their feet.
10027{_}1873{_}"The chain's gone," gasped the captain.
10027{_}1874{_}"There's a cart behind!
10027{_}1875{_}Quick, run her aground!"
10027{_}1876{_}Of course the mate turned the handle the wrong way.
10027{_}1877{_}On one side of the road was an ordinary hedge, while on the other lay a deep ditch, and into this a moment later the "coffee-mill" disappeared with every soul on board!
10027{_}1878{_}There was an awful moment, when earth, sky, arms, legs, wheels, and bushes seemed all mixed together, and then Jack Vance found himself resting on his hands and knees in a puddle of dirty water.
10027{_}1879{_}Diggory and Mugford had been driven with considerable violence into the thickest part of a thorn hedge, and proceeded to extricate themselves therefrom with many groans and lamentations.
10027{_}1880{_}"Well," said the mate, as they proceeded to drag the machine out of the ditch, "I should think, Jack, you've celebrated your birthday about enough; now you'd better give over, or we shall all be sent home in a sack."
10027{_}1881{_}"Me!"
10027{_}1882{_}cried the captain, with great indignation.
10027{_}1883{_}"It was _your_ fault, you dummy!
10027{_}1884{_}you put the helm over wrong again, you--"  "Hullo, you kids!"
10027{_}1885{_}interrupted a voice behind them, and turning round the three friends saw the burly form of John Acton pushing a bicycle up the hill.
10027{_}1886{_}"Hullo!"
10027{_}1887{_}he continued; "it's young Trevanock.
10027{_}1888{_}What's up?
10027{_}1889{_}Have you had a spill?"
10027{_}1890{_}"Yes; the chain broke, and we ran into the ditch."
10027{_}1891{_}"Umph!
10027{_}1892{_}bad business.
10027{_}1893{_}Now you'll have to foot it, I suppose."
10027{_}1894{_}"Yes," answered Jack ruefully; "and we're bound to be back late pushing this old thing all the way.
10027{_}1895{_}I wish old Jobling would try a ride on it himself."
10027{_}1896{_}"Oh!
10027{_}1897{_}is that the 'coffee-mill'?"
10027{_}1898{_}exclaimed the prefect, laughing.
10027{_}1899{_}"Well, look here!
10027{_}1900{_}If you're late, I'll see whoever's on duty, and tell him about the breakdown, and see if I can get you off."
10027{_}1901{_}"Oh, thanks awfully!"
10027{_}1902{_}chorused the small boys.
10027{_}1903{_}"I've half a mind to say I wouldn't," continued Acton, looking round as he put his foot on the step of his machine, and nodding his head at Diggory.
10027{_}1904{_}"I owe you a grudge for not telling me what I wanted to know about my young brother's love-letter."
10027{_}1905{_}The football captain was as good as his word: he got the Triple Alliance excused the "impot" which would otherwise have been awarded them for arriving at the school half an hour late, and the only misfortune which resulted from their eventful excursion was that Jack Vance had to expend a further portion of his postal order in paying Jobling for repairing the broken chain.
10027{_}1906{_}The day, however, did not close without another incident happening to one of the voyagers, which, though trifling in itself, proved, as it were, the shadow of coming events which were destined to seriously affect the well-being and happiness of all the Ronleigh boys.
10027{_}1907{_}Crossing the quadrangle soon after tea, Diggory saw something bright lying on the gravel; it proved to be a silver match-box with the letters C. T. engraved on the front.
10027{_}1908{_}He took it with him into the school-room, and holding it up as the boys were assembling at their desks for preparation, asked if any one knew who was the owner.
10027{_}1909{_}"Yes, I do," answered young Fletcher: "it's Thirsty's; I've seen it often."
10027{_}1910{_}Preparation of the next day's work having ended, Diggory's attention was occupied for a time in discussing with Carton the merits of some foreign stamps.
10027{_}1911{_}Just before supper, however, he remembered the match-box, and hurried away to restore it to its rightful owner.
10027{_}1912{_}Thurston was evidently at home, for a prolonged shout of laughter and the clamour of several voices reached Diggory's ears as he approached the study.
10027{_}1913{_}As he knocked at the door the noise suddenly ceased, there was a moment's silence, and then a murmur in a low tone, followed by a scuffling of feet and the overturning of a chair.
10027{_}1914{_}"Who's there?
10027{_}1915{_}you can't come in!"
10027{_}1916{_}shouted the owner of the den.
10027{_}1917{_}"I don't want to," answered Diggory, through the keyhole.
10027{_}1918{_}"I've brought your match-box that I picked up in the 'quad.'"
10027{_}1919{_}"Oh, it's only a kid," said the voice of Fletcher senior; and the next instant the door was unlocked by Thurston, who opened it about six inches, and immediately thrust his body into the aperture, as though to prevent the possibility of the visitor getting any sight of the interior of the room.
10027{_}1920{_}"Oh, thanks; you're a brick," he said, taking the box, and immediately closed the door and turned the key.
10027{_}1921{_}Diggory was retracing his steps along the passage, wondering what could be the object of all this secrecy, when he nearly ran into the school captain.
10027{_}1922{_}"Hullo, young man!"
10027{_}1923{_}said the latter, "where have you been?"
10027{_}1924{_}"To Thurston's study."
10027{_}1925{_}"What have you been there for?"
10027{_}1926{_}demanded Allingford sharply, with a sudden change in his tone and manner.
10027{_}1927{_}"Only to give him his match-box that I picked up in the 'quad.'"
10027{_}1928{_}The captain eyed the speaker narrowly, as though half inclined to doubt the truth of this explanation; then, apparently satisfied with the honest expression of the small boy's face, told him to get down to supper.
10027{_}1929{_}The latter wandered off, wondering more than ever what could have been the object of the private gathering in Thurston's study which he had just interrupted.
10027{_}1930{_}"It's what I told you before," remarked Carton, when Diggory chanced to mention what had happened.
10027{_}1931{_}"Thirsty's going to the dogs, and I believe big Fletcher's got a lot to do with it.
10027{_}1932{_}Allingford can't interfere with them as long as they keep to themselves.
10027{_}1933{_}I don't know what they do, but I shouldn't be surprised if there is a rare old kick-up one of these fine days."
10027{_}1934{_}Mischief certainly was brewing, and the "kick-up" came sooner than even Carton himself expected.
10027{_}1935{_}CHAPTER XII.
10027{_}1936{_}THE WRAXBY MATCH.
10027{_}1937{_}Wednesday, the twenty-fourth of July, saw the whole of Ronleigh College in a state of bustle and excitement.
10027{_}1938{_}The near approach of the holidays was sufficient in itself to put every one in high spirits, while, in addition to this, the afternoon was to witness the chief cricket contest of the season--the annual match against Wraxby Grammar School.
10027{_}1939{_}During the hour before dinner the ground itself was a scene of brisk activity: the school colours flew at the summit of the flagstaff; the boundary flags fluttered in the breeze; a number of willing hands, under the direction of Allingford, put a finishing touch to the pitch with the big roller, while others assisted in rigging up the two screens of white canvas in line with the wickets.
10027{_}1940{_}"I do hope we lick them," said little "Rats" to Jack Vance as they stood by the pavilion, watching Oaks mixing some whiting for the creases; "we _must_ somehow or other."
10027{_}1941{_}"Why?"
10027{_}1942{_}"Why?
10027{_}1943{_}because they've beaten us now three times running; and the last time when our chaps went over to Wraxby and got licked at footer their captain asked Ally if in future we should like to play a master!
10027{_}1944{_}Such rot!"
10027{_}1945{_}continued the youthful "Rats," boiling with wrath; "as if we couldn't smash them without!
10027{_}1946{_}Look here, I'd give--I'd give sixpence if we could win!"
