10027{_}1947{_}and with this burst of patriotic enthusiasm the speaker hurried away to join Maxton, who, with an old sprung racquet in one hand and the inside of an exploded cricket-ball in the other, was calling to him from the adjoining playing field to "Come and play tip and run, and bring something that'll do for a wicket."
10027{_}1948{_}The feelings expressed by "Rats" as regards the result of the match were shared by the whole school, and by none more so than the members of the Third Form.
10027{_}1949{_}"The Happy Family" turned up to a man, and encamped _en masse_ upon the turf within twenty yards of the pavilion.
10027{_}1950{_}Bibbs was the last to arrive on the scene of action, and did so with a bag of sweets in one hand, a book in the other, and a piece of paper, pinned by some joker to the tail of his coat, bearing the legend, "Please to kick me"--a request which was immediately responded to in a most hearty and generous fashion by all present.
10027{_}1951{_}Kicking the unfortunate Bibbs afforded every one such exquisite enjoyment that an effort was made to prolong the pastime by forcible attempts to fasten the placard on to other members of the company, and a general _melee_, would have followed if the attention of the combatants had not been attracted in another direction.
10027{_}1952{_}Ronleigh having won the toss and elected to go in first, the Wraxby men strolled out of the pavilion to take the field.
10027{_}1953{_}They were a likely-looking lot of fellows--the faded flannel caps and careless way in which they sauntered towards the pitch proclaiming the fact that each one was a veteran player.
10027{_}1954{_}"That chap with the wicket-keeping gloves in his hand is Partridge, their captain," said Carton; "and that fellow who's putting out the single stump to bowl at is Austin.
10027{_}1955{_}He does put them in to some tune; you can hardly see the ball, it's so swift."
10027{_}1956{_}There was a faint _clang_ from the pitch.
10027{_}1957{_}"See that!"
10027{_}1958{_}cried Fletcher junior: "that chap Austin's knocked that single stump out of the ground first ball.
10027{_}1959{_}My eye, he'll make our fellows sit up, I'll bet."
10027{_}1960{_}"No, he won't," cried "Rats" excitedly.
10027{_}1961{_}"Old Ally'll knock him into a cocked hat.
10027{_}1962{_}He'll soon break his back," added the speaker complaisantly.
10027{_}1963{_}"Hullo!
10027{_}1964{_}men in--Parkes and Rowland."
10027{_}1965{_}There is something in the short space of time preceding the first _clack_ of the bat at a cricket match which rivals in interest even that exciting moment at football when the centre forward stands hovering over the ball waiting for the whistle to give the signal for the contest to commence.
10027{_}1966{_}The noisy clatter of "The Happy Family" ceases as the crowd of boys, ranged all down the sides of the field, turn to watch the opening of the game.
10027{_}1967{_}It is an ideal day for cricket, with a fresh breeze blowing, just sufficient to temper the hot afternoon sunshine and cause a flutter of cricket-shirts and boundary flags.
10027{_}1968{_}Rowland takes centre, twists the handle of his bat round and round in his hands, and is heard amid the general hush to say, "No, no trial."
10027{_}1969{_}Austin glances round at the motionless figures of his comrades, signals to _long-on_ to stand a little deeper,  and then delivers the ball.
10027{_}1970{_}With an easy and graceful forward stroke, the batsman returns it sharply in the direction of the opposite wicket, and an almost imperceptible movement, like the releasing of a spring, takes place among the fielders.
10027{_}1971{_}So begins the battle.
10027{_}1972{_}"Twenty up!"
10027{_}1973{_}had just been called from the pavilion when a sharp catch in the slips disposed of Parkes.
10027{_}1974{_}"Never mind!"
10027{_}1975{_}cried "Rats."
10027{_}1976{_}"Here comes old Ally; he'll make them trot round a bit!"
10027{_}1977{_}The captain commenced his innings with a heart-warming leg hit, which sent the ball to the boundary, a wave of legs and arms marking its track as the spectators, with a joyous yell, rolled over one another to escape being hit.
10027{_}1978{_}For some time cheer followed cheer, and "The Happy Family" clapped until their hands smarted; then suddenly there arose a prolonged "_Oh, oh!_" from all the field.
10027{_}1979{_}"Hullo!
10027{_}1980{_}what's the matter?"
10027{_}1981{_}asked Bibbs, looking up from the book he was reading.
10027{_}1982{_}"What's the matter?"
10027{_}1983{_}shouted Maxton wrathfully, snatching away the volume and banging Bibbs on the head with it.
10027{_}1984{_}"Why don't you watch the game?
10027{_}1985{_}Old Ally's bowled off his pads!"
10027{_}1986{_}It was only too true: the captain's wicket was down, and "The Happy Family," after a simultaneous ejaculation of "_Blow it!_" tore up stalks of grass, and began to chew them with a stern expression on their faces.
10027{_}1987{_}This disaster seemed but the forerunner of others.
10027{_}1988{_}Redfern, the next man, had hardly taken his place at the wicket when a sharp _click_, the glitter of bails twirling in the air, and a Wraxby shout of "Well bowled!"
10027{_}1989{_}announced his fate; while ten minutes later Rowland, one of the mainstays of the home team, was caught in a most provoking manner at _cover-point_.
10027{_}1990{_}"Oh, bother it all!"
10027{_}1991{_}sighed "Rats; this is nothing but a procession."
10027{_}1992{_}"Now, Oaks, old chap, do your best for us!"
10027{_}1993{_}cried Allingford.
10027{_}1994{_}"All right," returned the other, laughing, as he paused for a moment outside the pavilion to fasten the strap of his batting-glove; "I'm going to make runs this journey, or die in the attempt."
10027{_}1995{_}Oaks was undoubtedly a regular Briton, just the sort of fellow to turn the fortunes of a losing game.
10027{_}1996{_}He walked up to the wicket as coolly as though it were enclosed within a practice net, patted down the ground with the flat of his bat in a manner which seemed to imply that he had "come to stay," and then proceeded to hit three twos in his first "over."
10027{_}1997{_}This dashing commencement was but the prelude to a brilliant bit of rapid scoring: twos and threes followed each other in quick succession.
10027{_}1998{_}Allingford shouted, the crowd roared, while "The Happy Family" gambolled about on one another's chests and stomachs, and squealed with delight.
10027{_}1999{_}Like the poet's brook, Oaks might have exclaimed, "Men may come, and men may go, but I go on for ever."
10027{_}2000{_}When Wraxby changed the bowling, he welcomed the new-comer by sending the first ball into the next field, and continued to cut and drive in such a gallant manner that even Bibbs, standing up to get the full use of his lungs, shouted, "Go 'long!"
10027{_}2001{_}and "Well hit!"
10027{_}2002{_}until his face was the colour of a poppy.
10027{_}2003{_}"I say!"
10027{_}2004{_}exclaimed Carton, as the eighth wicket fell, "I wish one of these next two chaps would hang on a bit, and give Oaks a chance of getting a few more; it must be nearly eighty up."
10027{_}2005{_}"Thurston, you're in!"
10027{_}2006{_}came from the scorer.
10027{_}2007{_}The boy named was sitting by himself, on the end of a form close to the telegraph, moodily scraping up the ground with the spikes of his cricket-shoes.
10027{_}2008{_}He knew that most of his comrades in the eleven would give him the cold shoulder, and so did not mingle with them inside the pavilion.
10027{_}2009{_}He rose, and prepared to obey the summons.
10027{_}2010{_}"Let's give him a cheer," said Rats; "he may do something.--Go it, Thurston!
10027{_}2011{_}Sit tight, and keep the pot boiling!"
10027{_}2012{_}The big fellow turned his head in the direction of "The Happy Family," and with something of the old good-humoured smile, which had seldom of late been seen upon his face, answered: "All right, my boy, you see if I don't."
10027{_}2013{_}"Jolly fellow old Thirsty," remarked "Rats," swelling with pride at this friendly recognition.
10027{_}2014{_}"He can play when he likes, but he hasn't troubled to practise much of late.
10027{_}2015{_}He used always--Phew!
10027{_}2016{_}my eye, what an awful crack!"
10027{_}2017{_}A terrifically swift ball from Austin had risen suddenly from the hard ground.
10027{_}2018{_}Thurston had no time to avoid it, but turning away his face, received the blow on the back of his head.
10027{_}2019{_}He dropped his bat, staggered away from the wicket, and fell forward on his knees.
10027{_}2020{_}To suffer for the cause of the school in a cricket or football match was a thing which, like charity, "covered a multitude of sins."
10027{_}2021{_}Allingford hurried out of the pavilion and ran towards the pitch, while Partridge and a few more of the "Wraxby men gathered round their wounded opponent and helped him to his feet.
10027{_}2022{_}"You'd better come out, Thurston," said the Ronleigh captain; "I'll send the next man in."
10027{_}2023{_}"No, I'll go on," replied the other, in rather a shaky voice; "I shall be all right in a minute."
10027{_}2024{_}It requires something more than ordinary pluck for a batsman to stand up to fast bowling and show good form after having been badly hit.
10027{_}2025{_}For a time a great deal of determination, and the exercise of a considerable amount of will power, are necessary to conquer the natural inclination to shrink from a possible repetition of the injury; and those who watched the dogged manner in which Thurston continued to defend his wicket, being themselves practical cricketers, rewarded him with loud shouts of encouragement and praise.
10027{_}2026{_}Oaks piled on the score with unflagging energy, while the careful play of his companion defied all attempts of the Wraxby bowlers to dissolve the partnership.
10027{_}2027{_}"Bravo, 'Thirsty!'"
10027{_}2028{_}shouted the spectators.
10027{_}2029{_}"Go 'long'--and another!"
10027{_}2030{_}At length, just as the telegraph operator had received the welcome order, "A hundred up!"
10027{_}2031{_}the ball shot, and crashed into Thurston's wicket.
10027{_}2032{_}He came slowly back from the pitch, still holding his hand to the back of his head; and though his individual score had barely run into double figures, he was greeted on all sides with hearty cheers.
10027{_}2033{_}Payne, the last man, just succeeded in cracking his _duck's-egg_, and the innings closed for 104.
10027{_}2034{_}As the fielders came trooping in, a small boy ran past the Third Form encampment exclaiming, "I say, you chaps, old Punch is in the lower road, over by that tree!"
10027{_}2035{_}Which announcement had no sooner been made than the greater part of "The Happy Family" sprang to their feet, and went scampering across the field in the direction of the opposite hedge.
10027{_}2036{_}The cause of this stampede, it must be explained, was the arrival of an itinerant vendor of ice-cream, whose real name, Samuel Jones, had been changed to Punch on account of the prominence of his nasal organ.
10027{_}2037{_}His presence within the grounds of Ronleigh College was not approved of by the authorities, and his trade with the small boys, who were his particular patrons, was carried on through a gap in the hedge.
10027{_}2038{_}Punch's establishment ran on four wheels, and was ornamented with a number of daubs representing Union Jacks and Royal Standards, which formed the framework of an alarming portrait of the Prince of Wales, from which adornment one might be led to suppose that on some previous occasion His Royal Highness had patronized the stall.
10027{_}2039{_}The ice-cream was shovelled out of a tin receptacle, and pasted in lumps on to the top of very shallow glasses, the standard price for which was one penny; and there being a scarcity of spoons, the customers usually devoured the delicacy in the same manner as a dog does a saucer of milk.
10027{_}2040{_}Cynical members of the upper classes at Ronleigh, who had ceased to patronize the stall, charged Punch with not being over-particular in washing the glasses, and of making the "stuff," as they called it, with cornflour instead of cream.
10027{_}2041{_}But the small boys were not fastidious; and as each one had two helpings, which they ate as slowly as possible to prolong the enjoyment, they were still refreshing themselves when the home team moved out to field.
10027{_}2042{_}"Look sharp!"
10027{_}2043{_}cried "Rats," giving Bibbs's elbow a sudden jerk which caused that worthy to plaster the end of his nose with the remains of his third ice.
10027{_}2044{_}"Come on!
10027{_}2045{_}let's see the beginning."
10027{_}2046{_}The second half of the game proved, if anything, more exciting than the first.
10027{_}2047{_}Two wickets fell before 10 appeared on the telegraph.
10027{_}2048{_}"Oh, we shall lick them easily!"
10027{_}2049{_}cried "Rats" jubilantly; while Fletcher junior gave vent to his feelings by handing Bibbs's bag of sweets round to the company.
10027{_}2050{_}But there were still some hard nuts to be cracked in the Wraxby team, and one soon appeared in Partridge, the captain.
10027{_}2051{_}Over after over went by, and the score rapidly increased: "Thirty up!"
10027{_}2052{_}--"Forty up!"
10027{_}2053{_}--"Fifty up!"
10027{_}2054{_}Two more wickets were taken; but Partridge seemed to have fairly got his eye in, and gave the home team as much leather-hunting as Oaks had provided for the visitors.
10027{_}2055{_}To make matters worse, Austin, arriving on the scene sixth man in, appeared to be also possessed with a determination to carry his bat; and though he was eventually run out by a sharp throw-in from square-leg, it was not until eighty runs had been registered for the Grammar School.
10027{_}2056{_}The closing scene of the game caused an amount of excitement unparalleled in the history of Ronleigh cricket.
10027{_}2057{_}As the last man of the Wraxby team went in to bat, the telegraph was changed from 90 to 100.
10027{_}2058{_}"Over" had just been called, and the invincible Partridge stepped forward to play, evidently making up his mind for another boundary hit.
10027{_}2059{_}Thurston had been put on to bowl at the top end, and stood ready to recommence the attack.
10027{_}2060{_}"Four to equal, five to beat," sighed "Rats."
10027{_}2061{_}"Bother it all, they're sure to win."
10027{_}2062{_}A cricket match needs to be very narrowly watched, or the spectator whose eye has strayed for a moment from the game misses some fine piece of play.
10027{_}2063{_}The incident which finished the contest between Ronleigh College and Wraxby Grammar School occupied barely three seconds of time; yet it was remembered and spoken about many years after those concerned in it had passed on to swell the ranks of the "old boys."
10027{_}2064{_}Partridge commenced the over with a hard, straight drive, and at the same instant Thurston gave a little jump into the air with his right arm stretched above his head.
10027{_}2065{_}The ball had passed like lightning between the wickets, and the spectators looked for a moment to see where it had gone; then a wild shriek of joy from "The Happy Family" rent the air,--  "_Caught!_"  It was true enough.
10027{_}2066{_}With a splendid one-handed catch Thurston had brought the well-fought contest to a close, and secured a victory for Ronleigh College.
10027{_}2067{_}This brilliant feat, coupled with the gallant manner in which he had continued his innings when hurt, and so enabled Oaks to run up the score, caused the black sheep of the Sixth Form to be regarded as the hero of the day.
10027{_}2068{_}Allingford shook him by the hand, and a noisy crowd hoisted him shoulder high and carried him three times round the quadrangle.
10027{_}2069{_}Thurston certainly had good reason to feel proud of the part he had played in the chief match of the season, and might in years to come have always looked back with pleasure on this twenty-fourth of July.
10027{_}2070{_}Unfortunately another event of a sadly different character was destined to make it a red-letter day in his career at Ronleigh.
10027{_}2071{_}The feeling of respect and good-will which his prowess in the field had awakened in the minds of his former friends afforded him a splendid opportunity for reassociating himself with all that was worthy and honourable in school life.
10027{_}2072{_}The chance no sooner presented itself, however, than it was flung away, and was lost for ever.
10027{_}2073{_}Evening preparation was over, and supper, an informal meal, attendance at which was not compulsory, was in progress.
10027{_}2074{_}The door of Thurston's study was once more locked on the inside, as it had been when Diggory went to return the match-box to its rightful owner.
10027{_}2075{_}Fletcher senior, Hawley, and Gull sat on three sides of the small table, while Thurston himself occupied the fourth.
10027{_}2076{_}"Hang it all!"
10027{_}2077{_}exclaimed the latter, throwing down a handful of playing cards upon the table, and pushing back his chair.
10027{_}2078{_}"I shan't play any more to-night; I've got no more tin."
10027{_}2079{_}"Oh, go on; I'll lend you some," answered Fletcher.
10027{_}2080{_}"I don't care whether I win or lose; it's only the game I play for."
10027{_}2081{_}As a matter of fact, Fletcher nearly always _did_ win, and was mightily displeased on the rare occasions when he lost.
10027{_}2082{_}"No; I've borrowed enough already," returned the other.
10027{_}2083{_}"I shan't be able to square up as it is till next term.
10027{_}2084{_}It's all very well for fellows like you three, who have rich people, and can write home any time for a fiver; but I'm not so flush of cash.--Look here, Gull, have you got that banjo?
10027{_}2085{_}Sing us a song."
10027{_}2086{_}"All right," answered Gull, reaching down and picking a small five-stringed instrument off the floor; "what'll you have?"
10027{_}2087{_}"Oh, something with a good swing to it.
10027{_}2088{_}I feel like kicking up a row."
10027{_}2089{_}Gull tuned up, struck a few chords, and then launched out into a rattling nigger song with an amount of "go" and clatter sufficient to inspire the hearer with an almost irresistible desire to get up and dance.
10027{_}2090{_}The three listeners shouted the chorus at the top of their voices, pounding the table with their fists by way of a sort of drum accompaniment.
10027{_}2091{_}Gull was just preparing to commence the fourth verse when there was a knock at the study door.
10027{_}2092{_}"Wait a jiff," said Thurston.--"Who's there?
10027{_}2093{_}What d'you want?"
10027{_}2094{_}"Why," came the answer, uttered in rather a drawling tone, "I wish you fellows wouldn't make so much row.
10027{_}2095{_}I can't possibly work.
10027{_}2096{_}Do be quiet."
10027{_}2097{_}"Oh, go to Bath!"
10027{_}2098{_}shouted Thurston.--"It's only that old stew-pot Browse," he added.
10027{_}2099{_}"The beggar's got the next study, and he's cramming up for some 'exam.'
10027{_}2100{_}--Go on, Gull."
10027{_}2101{_}The entertainment continued, and waxed more noisy than ever, the performers hammering the table with a ruler and two walking-sticks to add zest to the choruses.
10027{_}2102{_}Soon there came another interruption, very different in tone from the mild expostulation of the studious Browse.
10027{_}2103{_}The door was violently shaken, and from without came the sharp, peremptory order of the school captain,--  "Look here, Thurston, just shut up; we've had enough of this horrible row for one night.
10027{_}2104{_}Stop it, d'you hear?"
10027{_}2105{_}"All right," growled the owner of the study; "keep your hair on, old fellow!"
10027{_}2106{_}"Sh!
10027{_}2107{_}steady on, Thirsty," said Fletcher, in a low tone.
10027{_}2108{_}"Don't go too far, or he'll put a stop to our next merry meeting.
10027{_}2109{_}I know Allingford, and he's rather a hard wall to run your head against."
10027{_}2110{_}"That confounded old Browse has gone and sneaked!"
10027{_}2111{_}cried the other, with a flush of passion on his face.
10027{_}2112{_}"Let's wait till Ally's gone, and then make a raid on the old stew-pot."
10027{_}2113{_}Hawley and Gull sprang to their feet with a murmur of assent; Fletcher shrugged his shoulders and remained silent.
10027{_}2114{_}"What we'll do is this," continued Thurston.
10027{_}2115{_}"He sits with his back to the door.
10027{_}2116{_}I'll pop in first and throw this tablecloth over his head; then, while I hold him down, you chaps upset the things and put out the light.
10027{_}2117{_}Then we'll rush out all together, and he won't know for certain who did it."
10027{_}2118{_}Five minutes later the conspirators crept out into the passage, and tip-toed towards the door of the adjoining study.
10027{_}2119{_}Fletcher lingered behind, and, instead of following the expedition, stole softly away in the opposite direction.
10027{_}2120{_}Another moment, and the unfortunate Browse was struggling to rise from his chair, with his head enveloped in the tablecloth.
10027{_}2121{_}Hawley and Gull, following immediately in rear of their leader, sent the table, with its load of books and writing materials, over with a crash, threw the chairs into different corners of the room, and were about to scatter the contents of the bookcase over the floor, when Allingford suddenly burst into the room, and stood glaring round like an angry lion.
10027{_}2122{_}With one swing of his right arm he sent Thurston staggering against the wall, and then, stepping forward without an instant's hesitation, he dealt each of the other marauders a swinging box on the ear.
10027{_}2123{_}The two Fifth Form boys were big, strong fellows, and for a moment it seemed as though a stand-up fight would ensue.
10027{_}2124{_}The captain, however, followed up his attack with amazing promptness, and before his antagonists had time to think of resistance he had taken them both by the shoulders and sent them flying into the passage.
10027{_}2125{_}"There!"
10027{_}2126{_}he exclaimed.
10027{_}2127{_}"I'll teach you gentlemen to come playing pranks on Sixth Form studies.
10027{_}2128{_}What business have you got here, I should like to know?--As for you," continued the speaker, casting a scornful glance at the originator of the outrage, "I should have thought a fellow who's a prefect ought to know better than to go rioting with every scamp in the school."
10027{_}2129{_}Thurston's conduct on the cricket field had clearly proved him to be no coward.
10027{_}2130{_}He stood his ground, and returned Allingford's angry glances with a look of fierce defiance.
10027{_}2131{_}He attempted to make some reply, but somehow the words failed him, and turning on his heel he walked away to his own study.
10027{_}2132{_}"Confound that fellow Fletcher!"
10027{_}2133{_}he muttered between his teeth.
10027{_}2134{_}"He always takes precious good care to sneak away when there's any row on.
10027{_}2135{_}If it wasn't for that money I owe him, I'd punch his head."
10027{_}2136{_}Half an hour later there was a sharp rap at the door, and Allingford, Oaks, and Acton entered the room.
10027{_}2137{_}"Well," said Thurston, looking up with a frown from the book he was reading, "what d'you want now?
10027{_}2138{_}I don't remember asking you fellows to come and see me.
10027{_}2139{_}A chap can't call his study his own nowadays."
10027{_}2140{_}"No," answered Acton grimly.
10027{_}2141{_}"If a chap wants to work, a lot of blackguards come and wreck his furniture."
10027{_}2142{_}"Look here, Thurston," said the captain coldly, "we've no wish to stay here longer than we can help.
10027{_}2143{_}We've come simply to tell you this--that after what's happened to-night the prefects are determined that to-morrow morning you send in your resignation to the doctor."
10027{_}2144{_}"And supposing I don't choose to send in my resignation?"
10027{_}2145{_}returned the other.
10027{_}2146{_}"Then," answered the captain calmly, "we shall send it in for you."
10027{_}2147{_}There was a moment's silence; then Thurston rose from his chair, and closing his book flung it down with a bang upon the table.
10027{_}2148{_}"All right," he said; "I'll do it.
10027{_}2149{_}You fellows have been set against me from the first.
10027{_}2150{_}I know all about it, and before I leave this place I'll pay you out."
10027{_}2151{_}"I almost wish we'd left it till after the holidays," said Oaks, as the three prefects walked down the passage.
10027{_}2152{_}"No," said Allingford firmly; "if we hesitate, and the fellows see it, we're lost.
10027{_}2153{_}It must be done at once."
10027{_}2154{_}"Well, perhaps so," answered Oaks; "but I'll tell you this--Thurston means mischief.
10027{_}2155{_}I wish he was going to leave.
10027{_}2156{_}He won't forget this in a hurry, and my belief is we shall hear more about it next term."
10027{_}2157{_}CHAPTER XIII.
10027{_}2158{_}THE ELECTIONS.
10027{_}2159{_}Thurston's resignation, as might have been expected, gave rise to a considerable amount of excitement and conflicting opinion.
10027{_}2160{_}Nearly every boy in the school saw clearly that he was both unworthy and unfitted to fulfil the duties of a prefect, but the peculiar circumstances under which he had, as "Rats" put it, been given "notice to quit," caused a large number of his schoolfellows to side with him, and condemn the action of the captain.
10027{_}2161{_}Only a few of the general public knew exactly what the row had been.
10027{_}2162{_}The Sixth Form authorities, refusing to be catechized, would answer no questions; while the other side took good care to spread abroad a very one-sided account of the affair.
10027{_}2163{_}The Wraxby match was fresh in everybody's mind.
10027{_}2164{_}"Awfully hard lines I call it," said the cricketers.
10027{_}2165{_}"He won that game for us; why didn't they let him go on a few days more till the end of the term?"
10027{_}2166{_}While those young gentlemen, of whom a few are to be found in every school, who cherish a strong dislike to anything in the shape of law and order, were, of course, loud in their expressions of dissatisfaction at the removal of one who always winked at their transgressions.
10027{_}2167{_}At the commencement of the winter session it soon became evident that seven weeks of summer holiday had not dispelled the cloud which had overshadowed the close of the previous term.
10027{_}2168{_}No sooner had the first excitement of meeting and settling down subsided a little than the question of Thurston's deposal cropped up again, and caused an unusual amount of interest to be felt by all Ronleigh in the forthcoming elections.
10027{_}2169{_}Every school has its own methods of choosing those who are to fill the posts and offices in connection with its various institutions, and it will be well to describe, in a few words, how this was done at Ronleigh, in order that the reader may follow with greater interest the working out of an important event in the history of the college.
10027{_}2170{_}The elections took place twice a year--at the commencement of the summer and winter terms--their chief object being to appoint what was known as the Sports Committee (who had the management of athletics and of the forthcoming cricket or football season), two librarians, and a keeper of the reading-room.
10027{_}2171{_}In addition to this, when any of the prefects left, fresh ones were chosen in their places.
10027{_}2172{_}Only members of the Sixth Form were eligible for this office, which was not conferred before the choice of the boys had been confirmed by the sanction of the head-master, and was understood to last for the remainder of the recipient's school life.
10027{_}2173{_}On the second or third morning of the term a paper was posted up on the notice-board in the big schoolroom, announcing the fact that the elections would take place two days later, and mentioning exactly what each voter was required to do.
10027{_}2174{_}Every boy who had been two terms at the school received a voting paper, which he filled up at his leisure and handed over to the returning officers at a special assembly called for the purpose.
10027{_}2175{_}At the commencement of this particular winter term the school reassembled on a Tuesday, and on Thursday notice was given that the elections would take place on the following Saturday afternoon.
10027{_}2176{_}According to the usual custom, when fresh prefects were to be chosen, the names of all the Sixth Form boys who were not already holding that office were mentioned on the notice, to show who were eligible for the position.
10027{_}2177{_}Thurston's name did not appear on the list; some one added it in pencil, another hand crossed it out, and an hour or two later it was added again, this time in red ink.
10027{_}2178{_}This simple action seemed the signal for a general agitation on Thurston's behalf.
10027{_}2179{_}His friends throughout the school openly proclaimed their intention of voting for him, and exhorted others to do the same.
10027{_}2180{_}Almost to a man the Sixth and Remove sided with the captain, but Hawley and Gull in the Fifth, Noaks and Mouler in the Upper Fourth, and other fellows in the lower forms made up their minds to secure Thurston's return, and set to work to carry out their project with a zeal worthy of a better cause.
10027{_}2181{_}Two fresh prefects were required, and the friends of law and order were unanimous in naming Fielding and Parkes as the most suitable candidates to fill the vacancies.
10027{_}2182{_}Rival posters appeared on the double doors leading to the playground:--                REMEMBER THE WRAXBY MATCH,                           AND                    VOTE FOR THURSTON.
10027{_}2183{_}PLUMP FOR PARKES,                       AND HAVE A                     PROPER PREFECT.
10027{_}2184{_}But this method of carrying on the campaign was soon brought into disrepute, owing to the fact that certain juveniles, seeing in this new idea of bill-posting a fresh field for practical joking, began to adorn the walls of the "grub-room," and other spaces which did not often come under the eye of a master, with placards exhibiting inscriptions which had no bearing on the elections--such irrelevant remarks as, "nooks Two wants kicking !"
10027{_}2185{_}or, "_Lost_-my wits.
10027{_}2186{_}(Signed) B. BIBBS," being calculated to occasion a considerable amount of strife and bad blood without serving any useful purpose.
10027{_}2187{_}The Lower School was in a fever heat of excitement, and it is quite possible that the little pleasantries which have just been alluded to were occasioned by difference of opinion on the one absorbing topic of the day.
10027{_}2188{_}The close of the previous holidays had witnessed a general parliamentary election, and with the details of contests which had taken place in their native towns vividly impressed upon their minds, the younger boys, from the Lower Fourth downwards, threw themselves into the present conflict with an amount of energy and spirit which was not to be found in the more sober and deliberate action of their seniors.
10027{_}2189{_}The greater number of the old "Happy Family" had now been removed into the Lower Fourth, and this form in particular was rent with opposing views, and shaken with continued outbursts of hostility between the rival factions.
10027{_}2190{_}The Triple Alliance were loyal to the old _regime_, and were supported by "Rats," Carton, and several of their old friends.
10027{_}2191{_}"Acton saved us from getting into a row after that 'coffee-mill' business," remarked Diggory.
10027{_}2192{_}"Rowland gave Noaks a dressing down when he hit me in the mouth," said Jack Vance.
10027{_}2193{_}"And old Ally boxed Mouler's ears when they made me upset that paint," added Mugford.
10027{_}2194{_}"Rats" declared that he meant to conduct what he called a "house-to-house visitation," and accordingly, beginning at the bottom of the form, the first person he called upon was Grundy, a great lout of sixteen, who had been at the tail end of the Lower Fourth for the last twelve months.
10027{_}2195{_}As it happened, Grundy was a strong partisan of the opposite side, and not only refused to vote for Parkes, but, seizing hold of the unfortunate canvasser, proceeded to twist his arms and pinch his ears for daring to oppose the election of Thurston.
10027{_}2196{_}Fletcher Two, whose sympathies, as might have been expected, were with his brother's chum, organized open-air meetings in one corner of the field where the big cricket-roller could be used as a platform.
10027{_}2197{_}But here, again, the love of larking which is so characteristic of the lawless small boy came into evidence, and with that touch of nature which makes the whole world kin, friend and foe alike joined in the spree of interrupting the proceedings.
10027{_}2198{_}Just when the orator had reached the most important point in his harangue, and was pouring forth a torrent of impassioned eloquence, the platform would begin to move, or the audience would insist on turning the gathering into an imaginary "scrum," and almost crushing the life out of those who happened to be in the middle of the crowd.
10027{_}2199{_}Poor Bibbs especially became a target for the humour of the electors.
10027{_}2200{_}According to Fletcher's instructions, he had written out a speech and learned it by heart; but though he was being continually called upon to deliver it, he never got beyond the opening "Ahem!
10027{_}2201{_}Gentlemen," before a sudden movement of the platform precipitated him into the arms of his irreverent hearers, or a shout of "Play up at the cocoa-nuts!"
10027{_}2202{_}followed by a shower of acorns, bits of stick, and pieces of turf, caused him to jump down and hastily seek shelter behind the roller.
10027{_}2203{_}For two days, especially in the Lower School, the excitement continued steadily to increase, and small boys being seized in out-of-the-way corners were made to assert at one time that they would vote for Thurston, and at another that they would vote for Parkes or Fielding, and so, in order to escape with a whole skin, were forced to commit perjury at least a dozen times between the hours of breakfast and tea.
10027{_}2204{_}One incident, which as far as the Lower Fourth was concerned tended considerably to embitter the contest, is worthy of record as a notable feature of this memorable campaign.
10027{_}2205{_}The occupants of dormitory No.
10027{_}2206{_}13 were rabid Thurstonians; dormitory No.
10027{_}2207{_}14, on the other hand, in which slept the Triple Alliance, Maxton, "Rats," and Carton, were to a man supporters of Parkes and Fielding.
10027{_}2208{_}On Friday evening the two doors, which were exactly opposite to each other, being left open, the process of undressing was enlivened by a continual fire of abuse and insulting remarks, which might have led to a regular scrimmage between the two parties if the presence of the prefect, patrolling the passage, had not prevented either side from advancing beyond the threshold of their own doorway.
10027{_}2209{_}"I wouldn't vote for a chap like Thurston, who goes boozing in a common 'pub' like the Black Swan," cried "Rats;" "but that's just the sort of man for you.
10027{_}2210{_}You're a cheap lot, the whole crew of you!"
10027{_}2211{_}"Look here, young 'Rats,'" retorted Fletcher junior from the opposite room, wandering rather wide of the subject in hand.
10027{_}2212{_}"Why don't you write home and ask your people to buy you a new pair of braces, instead of mending those old ones up with string?
10027{_}2213{_}You look just like a young street arab, and that's about what you are!"
10027{_}2214{_}"Don't you fellows talk about broken braces, and looking like street arabs," cried Diggory, "when only yesterday old Greyling sent Stokes out of class and told him to go down to the lavatory and wash his face.
10027{_}2215{_}That's a sample of you Thurstonians!"
10027{_}2216{_}"Look here!"
10027{_}2217{_}shouted the boy alluded to, springing out of bed, and appearing in his night-shirt at the opposite end of the dormitory.
10027{_}2218{_}"You know very well that Grundy flipped a pen full of ink over me, and that was why I had to go out and wash my face."
10027{_}2219{_}"I know you looked altogether a different fellow when you came back," returned Jack Vance: "I hardly knew you!"
10027{_}2220{_}There was a momentary pause in the discussion, and Bibbs, thinking this a suitable opportunity for the delivery of his speech, stepped forward, and took up his stand in the doorway.
10027{_}2221{_}Hardly, however, had he pronounced the opening "Ahem!
10027{_}2222{_}Gentlemen," when a cake of soap, flung by Maxton, struck him a violent blow in the pit of the stomach, and he was still rolling and groaning on his bed in the throes of recovering his lost wind when the prefect arrived to turn out the light.
10027{_}2223{_}The occupants of the two dormitories lay down, but not to sleep.
10027{_}2224{_}"You mark my word," said Diggory, "as soon as the prefects have gone down to supper those chaps from over the way'll come across and pay us out for throwing that soap.
10027{_}2225{_}We'd better put a chair against the door."
10027{_}2226{_}"Look here!"
10027{_}2227{_}remarked Fletcher junior to his room-mates.
10027{_}2228{_}"I shouldn't be at all surprised if Maxton and those other fellows in No.
10027{_}2229{_}14 come over and try to rag us; let's lie awake a bit and listen."
10027{_}2230{_}For half an hour all was quiet and still, and the watchers in No.
10027{_}2231{_}14 were turning over and preparing to go to sleep, when "Rats" started up, exclaiming in a whisper, "They're coming!
10027{_}2232{_}I heard some one in the passage.
10027{_}2233{_}There 'tis again!
10027{_}2234{_}Jump up, you chaps, and let's make a sortie."
10027{_}2235{_}Now, strange to say, an exactly similar alarm had just been given by Fletcher junior in No.
10027{_}2236{_}13, and the reason was simply as follows:-- Mr. Greyling, the master of the Lower Fourth, in walking towards his bedroom in slippered feet, was seized with a sneezing fit, and halting just outside the two dormitories, gave vent to his feelings with a loud "Et-chow!"
10027{_}2237{_}After a moment's pause he sneezed again, and had hardly done so before both doors were suddenly flung open, and with a cry of "Ah, you sneaks!"
10027{_}2238{_}and another of "Come on, you blackguards!"
10027{_}2239{_}a crowd of white-robed figures rushed out, brandishing pillows and startling Mr. Greyling to such a degree that he exclaimed "Great Scott!"
10027{_}2240{_}and dropped his candle.
10027{_}2241{_}What followed is too sad to be related in detail.
10027{_}2242{_}Mr. Greyling scattered largess in the shape of lines among the crowd, and the next day the occupants of the two dormitories went about thirsting for each other's blood.
10027{_}2243{_}On Saturday, just before morning school, the voting papers were collected, and directly after dinner the boys assembled to hear the result of the poll.
10027{_}2244{_}According to the usual custom, no masters were present.
10027{_}2245{_}Allingford presided, and the excitement was intense.
10027{_}2246{_}A hush of expectation fell on the crowded room as the captain mounted the platform on which stood the head-master's desk.
10027{_}2247{_}Up to the present time elections at Ronleigh had been little more than a matter of form, but on this occasion every one felt that something more was at stake than the mere distribution of the school offices.
10027{_}2248{_}"Gentlemen, the business of this meeting, as you are very well aware, is to announce the result of the elections.
10027{_}2249{_}"The following," continued Allingford, referring to the paper which he held in his hand, "have been chosen to act as the Sports Committee: Myself chairman, Oaks, Acton, Rowland, Parkes, Redfern, and Hoyle.
10027{_}2250{_}"The two former librarians, Clarkson and Lang, have been re-elected.
10027{_}2251{_}"Dale, who for some time past held the position of keeper of the reading-room, having left, the choice of a successor has fallen between Lucas and Ferris, who, singularly enough, both received the same number of votes.
10027{_}2252{_}Each of these gentlemen being equally ready to withdraw in the other's favour, I exercised my prerogative as captain of the school, and gave the casting vote in favour of Lucas."
10027{_}2253{_}At this there was a slight murmur among the audience, though whether of dissent or approval it was impossible to tell.
10027{_}2254{_}The interruption was only momentary, for every one was too much interested in the next announcement to care much what became of the post of keeper of the reading-room.
10027{_}2255{_}"As you all know, two vacancies have occurred among the prefects, to fill which the following gentlemen have been chosen, and their election duly sanctioned by the head-master: Parkes and Fielding."
10027{_}2256{_}The words had hardly passed the speaker's lips when the whole room was in an uproar.
10027{_}2257{_}Cheers, howls, whistling, and the stamping of feet filled the air with an indescribable din; members of the Lower Fourth fought one another across the desks; and it was some minutes before Allingford could obtain sufficient silence to enable him to finish his speech.
10027{_}2258{_}"This," he said, in conclusion, "is the result of the present election.
10027{_}2259{_}I believe there has been some little difference of opinion among you, especially in regard to the selection of the two fresh prefects; there are so many worthy fellows in the Sixth that one can hardly wonder at your finding some difficulty in making your choice.
10027{_}2260{_}One thing is certain--namely, that the two gentlemen who have been elected to what is and always has been a very honourable position at Ronleigh are eminently fitted for the work.
10027{_}2261{_}The duties of a prefect are often difficult, and the reverse of pleasant; but I think you will agree with me when I say that in any large school it is eminently satisfactory to find that a certain amount of the government and discipline can be entrusted to the boys themselves, and I feel sure that you will give Parkes and Fielding the same willing support as you have always accorded to myself and the other prefects."
10027{_}2262{_}As the captain finished speaking, Hawley, Gull, Noaks, and several other boys sprang to their feet, their appearance being the signal for a fresh outburst of cheers and groans.
10027{_}2263{_}Young "Rats" commenced to hiss like a small steam-engine, while Grundy made frantic but futile attempts to reach over from the desk behind and smite him on the head with a French dictionary.
10027{_}2264{_}"If any one wishes to speak," said the chairman, "he is at liberty to do so; but, of course, we can't have more than one at a time."
10027{_}2265{_}With the exception of Hawley, those who had risen sat down again.
10027{_}2266{_}"I want to ask," said the former, "what were the numbers in the voting for the prefects?"
10027{_}2267{_}"Parkes received fifty-six votes, and Fielding forty-eight."
10027{_}2268{_}"Did Thurston receive any votes?"
10027{_}2269{_}"Yes."
10027{_}2270{_}"How many?"
10027{_}2271{_}"That," returned the captain, "is a question which, for certain reasons, I think it would be best not to answer."
10027{_}2272{_}"I think," interrupted Gull, rising to his feet, amid a murmur of excitement, "that we have a perfect right to insist on the figures being made public; everything in connection with these elections ought to be fair and open."
10027{_}2273{_}"I don't think," answered Allingford quietly, "that any one has ever had reason to accuse me of being unfair in any of my dealings; it is exactly because I think it would be hardly fair to Thurston himself that I propose not to publish the number of votes awarded to unsuccessful candidates."
10027{_}2274{_}The subject of this remark sat in the front row but one, lolling back against the desk behind, with his hands in his pockets and a sneering smile on his lips.
10027{_}2275{_}"I don't care what you do," he exclaimed, with a short laugh.
10027{_}2276{_}"I can guess pretty well what's coming."
10027{_}2277{_}"There!"
10027{_}2278{_}cried Gull; "you hear what Thurston says.
10027{_}2279{_}Now let's have the figures."
10027{_}2280{_}"Very well," answered the captain.
10027{_}2281{_}"If you insist, you shall have them.
10027{_}2282{_}The number of votes for Thurston was sixty-one."
10027{_}2283{_}"Then, if he got more votes than either Parkes or Fielding, why isn't he elected?"
10027{_}2284{_}"Because the doctor would not sanction it.
10027{_}2285{_}The names have to be submitted to him for approval, and he appointed Parkes and Fielding."
10027{_}2286{_}"Did you try to influence him to overlook Thurston?"
10027{_}2287{_}demanded Gull angrily.
10027{_}2288{_}But an immediate outburst of such cries as "Shame!"
10027{_}2289{_}"Shut up!"
10027{_}2290{_}and "Sit down!"
10027{_}2291{_}showed the speaker he had gone too far, and rendered it unnecessary for Allingford to reply to the question.
10027{_}2292{_}"I think," said Fletcher senior, rising to his feet when this interruption had ceased, and looking round with a foxy smile on his face, "that, with all due respect to the gentlemen who have been elected as prefects, it is a great pity that the doctor should not have consented to confirm the choice of the school, and reappoint Thurston.
10027{_}2293{_}I think if the matter were laid before him in a proper light he might be induced to reconsider his decision."
10027{_}2294{_}"Well, will you go and see him about it yourself?"
10027{_}2295{_}asked Allingford, with a slight sneer.
10027{_}2296{_}"No; of course I shouldn't go alone," returned Fletcher.
10027{_}2297{_}"I think it's a matter that should be taken up by the whole school."
10027{_}2298{_}There was a moment's lull in the proceedings, broken only by a confused murmur of voices; then Acton jumped to his feet.
10027{_}2299{_}The football captain was popular with everybody, and the sight of his jovial face and sturdy figure was greeted with a burst of cheers.
10027{_}2300{_}"Look here, you fellows," he began.
10027{_}2301{_}"I'm no speaker, but I can say enough to serve the purpose.
10027{_}2302{_}I think we are very much indebted to our captain, not only for presiding over this meeting, but for what he has done and is always doing for the good of the school.
10027{_}2303{_}I remember Ronleigh when it wasn't such a decent place as it is to-day.
10027{_}2304{_}A lot of things went on here when I was a kid that wouldn't be put up with now, and I don't think the school ever played such good games of cricket and football as we see at the present time.
10027{_}2305{_}A lot of this, you may take my word for it, is due to our captain, and I think we can't show our appreciation of his work in a better way than by giving him three cheers.
10027{_}2306{_}Now, then, take the time from me.
10027{_}2307{_}Three cheers for Allingford.
10027{_}2308{_}HIP, HIP, HURRAH!"
10027{_}2309{_}The big assembly shouted till the roof rang and the windows rattled; then the meeting slowly dispersed, a feeble attempt to raise three cheers for Thurston being met with as many groans as plaudits.
10027{_}2310{_}CHAPTER XIV.
10027{_}2311{_}A PASSAGE OF ARMS.
10027{_}2312{_}The Triple Alliance, in common with the rest of their schoolfellows, little thought, on returning from their summer holidays, what a memorable epoch the coming term would prove in the history of Ronleigh College; still less did any one imagine what important results would arise from the action of the three friends, and how much would depend on the loyalty of these youngsters for their _Alma Mater_.
10027{_}2313{_}They settled down to enjoy a peaceful thirteen weeks of work and play.
10027{_}2314{_}Jack Vance reported that the robbery of "the Hermit's" coins was regarded at Todderton as quite a piece of ancient history; and as Noaks appeared to have forgotten the existence of the clasp-knife, and, growing every day more intimate with Thurston and Co., seemed more than ever inclined to go his way and leave his former foes alone, the latter made up their minds to banish dull care, and consider their unfortunate misadventure as a storm which they had safely weathered.
10027{_}2315{_}The wave of excitement caused by the elections soon passed over.
10027{_}2316{_}The new prefects entered upon their duties, and in the performance of the same apparently met with no ill-will or opposition; yet to every keen observer it was evident that the recent contest had left behind it a distinct under-current of dissatisfaction, and for the first time in the memory of all concerned Ronleigh was a house divided against itself--no longer united in a common cause, but split into two factions, one pulling against the other, thinking more of party interests than of the honour and welfare of the whole community.
10027{_}2317{_}The first occasion on which this spirit clearly manifested itself was some ten days after the elections, when the college played their first football match of the season against Ronleigh town.
10027{_}2318{_}Thurston's name had, as usual, been included in the list of the eleven which was posted up on Wednesday morning, but before school was over it was noised abroad that he had refused to play.
10027{_}2319{_}"I say, you fellows, have you heard about 'Thirsty'?"
10027{_}2320{_}said Fletcher junior, as the Lower Fourth straggled into their classroom after interval.
10027{_}2321{_}"I wonder if it's true."
10027{_}2322{_}"Oh, it's true enough," answered Grundy from the back desk; "and I'm jolly glad he's done it.
10027{_}2323{_}I heard him say this morning that if Allingford and those other fellows wouldn't put up with him as a prefect, they shouldn't have him in the team."
10027{_}2324{_}"Well, I call that rot," cried Jack Vance: "the team doesn't belong to Allingford or to anybody else--"  "Oh, shut your mouth, you young prig!"
10027{_}2325{_}interrupted Grundy, and the entrance of Mr. Greyling put a stop to any further conversation.
10027{_}2326{_}I am inclined to think that a much nobler spirit would pervade such field-sports as cricket and football if the fact could be more firmly impressed upon the minds of both players and spectators that, providing the conduct of each side is fair and generous, and that every one does his "big best," it is equally creditable to lose as to win.
10027{_}2327{_}Certainly both sides should strive their hardest to gain the day; but let boys especially remember, in an uphill game, when scoring goes against them, that it is to the honour of the slaughtered Spartans and not of the victorious Persians that the pass of Thermopylae has become a household word.
10027{_}2328{_}In addition to the loss of Thurston, who, to do him justice, was a very good forward, the school team was weakened still further by an unfortunate accident which befell Rowlands, who twisted his ankle, and was forced to leave the ground at the very commencement of the game.
10027{_}2329{_}The Town were unusually strong, and the bulk of the back work fell on Allingford.
10027{_}2330{_}The captain played a magnificent game, and covered himself with glory; but in spite of all that he and his men could do, after a gallant fight the visitors claimed the victory with a score of four goals to two.
10027{_}2331{_}On the morning after the match, just before school, the members of the Triple Alliance were strolling across the entrance-hall, when they noticed a crowd of boys surrounding the notice-board.
10027{_}2332{_}The gathering seemed to consist mainly of members of the lower classes, and the manner in which they were elbowing each other aside, laughing, talking, and gesticulating, showed that some announcement of rather uncommon interest and importance must be exposed to view.
10027{_}2333{_}Our three friends hurried forward to join the group.
10027{_}2334{_}Pinned to the board with an old pen-nib was a half-sheet of scribbling-paper, and inscribed thereon, in what was evidently a disguised handwriting, were some verses, which were seen at once to refer to the previous afternoon's defeat.
10027{_}2335{_}They were as follows:--                      COLLEGE V. TOWN.
10027{_}2336{_}_Air_, "Bonnie Dundee."
10027{_}2337{_}To the boys of the college 'twas Allingford spoke:           "When we play the Town team there are heads to be broke;           So let ten veteran players come now follow me,           And fight for the honour of ancient Ronleigh."
10027{_}2338{_}_Chorus_.
10027{_}2339{_}"Then put up your goal-posts, and mark your touch-line;              We'll grind them to powder, and put them in brine.
10027{_}2340{_}Let boarders and day boys all come out to see              Us fight for the honour of ancient Ronleigh."
10027{_}2341{_}The ten merry men mustered quick at his call--           There were forwards, and half-backs, and goal-keeper tall;           But one who was wont in the forefront to be           No longer was seen in the ranks of Ronleigh.
10027{_}2342{_}_Chorus_: "Then put up your goal-posts, and mark," etc.
10027{_}2343{_}Too soon their rejoicings and empty their boast,           For the Town fellows very soon had them on toast;           And the bystanders sighed as they saw frequently           The ball pass the "back" of our ancient Ronleigh.
10027{_}2344{_}_Chorus_: "Then put up your goal-posts, and mark," etc.
10027{_}2345{_}From this draw a moral, you fellows who rule:           Sink personal spite when you act for the school;           And whatever your notions of prefects may be,           Let's have the right men in the team at Ronleigh.
10027{_}2346{_}_Chorus_: "Then put up your goal-posts, and mark," etc.
10027{_}2347{_}Something in these doggerel lines excited Jack Vance's wrath above measure, the last verse especially raising his anger to boiling-point, so that it fairly bubbled over.
10027{_}2348{_}Jack was a loyal-hearted youngster; he was nothing to Allingford, but Allingford was something to him, as head and leader of the community of which he himself was a member.
10027{_}2349{_}The sight of the captain toiling manfully through the long, unequal contest of the previous afternoon, doing practically double work to make up for the loss of his fellow-back, and to prevent a losing game degenerating into a rout, rose up once more before the small boy's mind, and, as has been said before, his wrath boiled over.
10027{_}2350{_}"Well, I call that a beastly shame.
10027{_}2351{_}The chap who wrote it ought to be kicked round the field."
10027{_}2352{_}"My eye," cried Grundy, "listen to what's talking!
10027{_}2353{_}Kicked round the field, indeed!
10027{_}2354{_}Why, I think it's jolly good: it serves Allingford and those other fellows just right for turning Thurston out of the team."
10027{_}2355{_}"What a lie!"
10027{_}2356{_}retorted Jack.
10027{_}2357{_}"You know very well they didn't turn him out; he went out of his own accord."
10027{_}2358{_}"Here, don't give me any of your cheek," said Grundy, sidling up to his antagonist in a threatening manner; "you mean to say I'm a liar, eh?"
10027{_}2359{_}The advent of three Fifth Form boys--one of whom took Grundy by the shoulders and pushed him away, with the command to "Get out and lie on the mat"--put an end, for the time being, to the altercation.
10027{_}2360{_}The crowd increased: boys of all ages stopped to read the verses; some few laughed, and pronounced them jolly good; but to do them justice, the greater number of Ronleians were too jealous of the honour of their school to see much fun in this attempt to lampoon their football representatives.
10027{_}2361{_}Just as the bell was ringing for assembly, the paper was torn down by Trail, the head of the Remove, who ripped it up into fifty pieces, and in answer to Gull's inquiry what he did that for, replied, "I'll jolly soon show you!"
10027{_}2362{_}in such a menacing tone that the questioner saw fit to turn on his heel and walk away with an alacrity of movement not altogether due to any particular eagerness to commence work.
10027{_}2363{_}The Lower Fourth were straggling down the passage on the way to their classroom, when they heard a scuffle and the clatter of falling books.
10027{_}2364{_}Grundy had seized Jack Vance by the collar from behind, and was screwing his knuckle into his victim's neck.
10027{_}2365{_}"Yes; you called me a liar, didn't you?"
10027{_}2366{_}"So you are!
10027{_}2367{_}Let go my coat!"
10027{_}2368{_}"Oh, so you stick to it, do you?
10027{_}2369{_}I'll--"  The sentence was interrupted by Jack giving a sudden twist and striking his antagonist a heavy blow in the chest, which sent him staggering against the opposite wall.
10027{_}2370{_}Grundy was nearly a head taller than Vance; but the latter's blood was up, and in another moment the dogs of war would have assuredly broken loose had not the flutter of a gown at the end of the passage announced the advent of Mr. Greyling.
10027{_}2371{_}The class had finished translating from their Latin author, and had just commenced writing an exercise, when a note was passed over to Jack Vance from the desk behind; it was short and to the point:--  "Will you fight me after twelve at the back of the pavilion?-- H.
10027{_}2372{_}GRUNDY."
10027{_}2373{_}Jack read the challenge, turned round and nodded, and then went calmly on with his work as though nothing had happened.
10027{_}2374{_}This cool way of treating the matter did not altogether please Grundy, who had rather expected that his adversary would elect to "take a licking."
10027{_}2375{_}He had, however, every reason to count upon an easy victory, and so promptly despatched another note, which contained the words: "Very well.
10027{_}2376{_}I'll smash you."
10027{_}2377{_}Later on a third epistle was handed over: "Don't tell any one, or there'll be too much of a crowd."
10027{_}2378{_}It was not until the interval that the two other members of the Triple Alliance were informed of the coming conflict.
10027{_}2379{_}"You don't really mean you're going to fight him?"
10027{_}2380{_}said Mugford.
10027{_}2381{_}"Of course I am."
10027{_}2382{_}"You'll get licked!"
10027{_}2383{_}added Diggory, with a sigh.
10027{_}2384{_}"I don't care if I am.
10027{_}2385{_}If I land him one or two, he won't be in a hurry to lick me again.
10027{_}2386{_}Don't you remember what you said ages ago at The Birches, Diggy, when you went down that slide on skates?
10027{_}2387{_}Well, it's the same thing with me now.
10027{_}2388{_}I'm going to show him, once and for all, that he's not going to ride rough-shod over me for nothing."
10027{_}2389{_}During the last hour of school, which happened to be devoted to algebra, the only member of the Triple Alliance who seemed able to work was Jack Vance.
10027{_}2390{_}Diggory made a hash of nearly every sum, while Mugford simply collapsed, and could not even remember that like signs made _plus_, and unlike _minus_.
10027{_}2391{_}"I say, Diggy," whispered the latter, "don't you think Grundy'll lick him?"
10027{_}2392{_}"I don't know," returned the other, with a desperate attempt to be cheerful; "you never know what may happen.
10027{_}2393{_}He may--"  "Trevanock, stop talking," interrupted Mr. Greyling.
10027{_}2394{_}"If I have to speak to you again for inattention, you'll stay in and work out these examples after twelve."
10027{_}2395{_}At length the faint jangle of the bell announced the fact that the eventful hour had arrived: the Lower Fourth passed on into the big schoolroom, and were dismissed with the other classes.
10027{_}2396{_}Jack betrayed not the least sign of excitement, and insisted on going down into the grub-room to feed two white mice before setting out for the "front."
10027{_}2397{_}His two friends, however, weighed down with anxiety, and with dismal forebodings as to the result of the coming conflict, were obliged to seek support by informing "Rats" of what was about to take place, and begging him to give them the benefit of his cheering company.
10027{_}2398{_}Young "Rats," who was always ready to take part in anything from a garden party to a game of marbles, immediately accepted the invitation.
10027{_}2399{_}"Jolly glad you told me," he cried; "wouldn't have missed seeing it for anything.
10027{_}2400{_}Jack Vance and Grundy--whew-w-w!"
10027{_}2401{_}The long whistle with which he concluded the sentence had certainly an ominous sound, but the appearance of their principal was the signal for the seconds to hide their fears under an assumed air of jovial confidence.
10027{_}2402{_}"You'll be certain to lick him, Jack," said Diggory, with a face as long as a fiddle;--"won't he, 'Rats'?"
10027{_}2403{_}"Lick him!"
10027{_}2404{_}answered "Rats;" "I should think so!
10027{_}2405{_}Lick him into fits; I could do it myself."
10027{_}2406{_}"He's a beastly bully," added Mugford solemnly; "and bullies always get licked--in books."
10027{_}2407{_}"I don't care," answered Jack jauntily, "if I lick him or not, but I know he'll find me a pretty hard nut to crack."
10027{_}2408{_}Ronleigh had no recognized duelling-ground, but when a premeditated encounter did take place, the combatants usually resorted to a little patch of grass situated between the back of the pavilion and the edge of the adjoining field.
10027{_}2409{_}Here it was possible to conduct an affair of honour without much fear of interruption.
10027{_}2410{_}Grundy was already at the trysting-place, accompanied by Andson, a chum from the Upper Fourth, and Fletcher junior.
10027{_}2411{_}It was quite an informal little gathering, and the business was conducted in a free-and-easy manner, and with an entire absence of the cut-and-dried ceremony which characterized similar undertakings in the palmy days of the prize ring.
10027{_}2412{_}"Look here, young Vance," said Grundy, "if you like to apologize for calling me a liar, I'll let you off; if not, I'm going to punch your head."
10027{_}2413{_}"Punch away!"
10027{_}2414{_}answered Jack stolidly, and all further attempt at pacification was abandoned.
10027{_}2415{_}The principals took off their coats and collars, while their companions drew aside to give them room, and the signal was given to commence the action.
10027{_}2416{_}Grundy made no attempt at any display of science; he simply relied on his superior strength and size, and charged down upon his adversary with the intention of thumping and pounding him till he gave in.
10027{_}2417{_}Jack Vance knew very little about the "noble art," except that it was the proper thing to hit straight from the shoulder; and following out this fundamental principle, he succeeded in landing his opponent a good hard drive between the eyes, which made him see more stars than are to be witnessed at the explosion of a sixpenny rocket.
10027{_}2418{_}Grundy drew back, and after blinking and rubbing his nose for a moment, came on again, this time with greater caution.
10027{_}2419{_}Jack, on the other hand, emboldened by his previous success, made an unwise attempt to rush the fighting, and was rewarded with a sounding smack on the cheek-bone which broke the skin and sent him staggering back into the arms of Diggory.
10027{_}2420{_}Once more the combatants approached each other, this time with a little more feinting and dodging, which showed a certain amount of respect for the weight of each other's fists.
10027{_}2421{_}At length, urged on to further feats of arms by impatient ejaculations of "Now, then, go into it!"
10027{_}2422{_}and "Keep the game alive!"
10027{_}2423{_}from Fletcher and Andson, they closed again, and after a sharp interchange of rather random pounding, Jack smote his opponent on the nose, and received in return a heavy blow on the chest which very nearly sent him to the ground.
10027{_}2424{_}After this there was another short breathing-space; a thin stream of blood was trickling from Grundy's nasal organ, while Diggory and Mugford noticed with aching hearts that their comrade was beginning to look rather limp, and was getting short of breath.
10027{_}2425{_}What would have been the ultimate result of the contest had it been resumed I am sure I cannot say, but I fear that, taking Grundy's superior weight and height into consideration, the story of the fight would have been recorded among the trials and not the triumphs of the Triple Alliance.
10027{_}2426{_}As it was, a sudden interruption brought the encounter to a premature close.
10027{_}2427{_}"Hullo, you young beggars!
10027{_}2428{_}what are you up to?"
10027{_}2429{_}The voice was that of Allingford, who, attracted by cries of "Go it!"
10027{_}2430{_}-- "Give him another!"
10027{_}2431{_}--"Bravo, Vance!"
10027{_}2432{_}and other warlike shouts, had hurried round to the rear of the pavilion to find out what was happening.
10027{_}2433{_}"Hullo!"
10027{_}2434{_}he continued, stepping forward and grasping Grundy by the shoulder; "what's up?
10027{_}2435{_}what's the joke?"
10027{_}2436{_}"It's only a bit of a fight," said Andson; "they had a row this morning."
10027{_}2437{_}"What, d'you mean to say you're fighting that youngster?
10027{_}2438{_}Why don't you choose some one a bit smaller?"
10027{_}2439{_}demanded the captain, rather bitterly.
10027{_}2440{_}"Well, it's his own doing," growled Grundy.
10027{_}2441{_}"I offered to let him off, but he wanted to have it out."
10027{_}2442{_}"Pshaw!"
10027{_}2443{_}returned the other.
10027{_}2444{_}"Look here, I've half a mind to give you two a jolly good 'impot' to keep you out of mischief.
10027{_}2445{_}Now stop it, d'you hear, or I'll send both your names in to Denson."
10027{_}2446{_}Fletcher and Andson had already beaten a retreat, and Grundy was preparing to follow, when Allingford called him back.
10027{_}2447{_}"Come," he said, in a kinder tone.
10027{_}2448{_}"I don't know what your quarrel's about, but finish it up like men, and shake hands."
10027{_}2449{_}The boys did as they were told, and though the salutation was not a very hearty one, it helped to extinguish the smouldering sparks of anger which might at some future meeting have been once more fanned into a flame.
10027{_}2450{_}Grundy disappeared round the corner of the building; but Allingford remained for a moment or two, watching Jack Vance as he fastened on his collar and resumed his coat.
10027{_}2451{_}"Well, what was the row about?"
10027{_}2452{_}"Oh, nothing."
10027{_}2453{_}"Nonsense; fellows don't fight for nothing.
10027{_}2454{_}What was it?
10027{_}2455{_}Any great secret?"
10027{_}2456{_}"Oh no," answered Jack, laughing: "it began about that lot of verses that was pinned upon the notice-board this morning.
10027{_}2457{_}Grundy said Thurston was turned out of the team, and I said he wasn't."
10027{_}2458{_}The captain smiled thoughtfully, and going down on one knee examined the wounded cheek.
10027{_}2459{_}"Put some cold water to it," he said, and then walked away.
10027{_}2460{_}That look was worth fifty bruises, and for it Jack would have continued the fight with Grundy to the bitter end.
10027{_}2461{_}Diggory and Mugford fell upon his neck, and were loud in their declarations that in another round their champion would have "knocked the stuffing out" of his opponent.
10027{_}2462{_}That this would really have been the case is, as I remarked before, rather doubtful; but one fact is certain--that the conflict caused the three friends to be more firmly established than ever in their loyalty to the side of law and order.
10027{_}2463{_}For a couple of days fellows continued to talk about the skit on the eleven, and to hazard guesses as to who was the writer.
10027{_}2464{_}As the majority, however, pronounced it "a dirty shame," and spoke of the author as "some mean skunk," the poet wisely concluded to conceal his identity, and by the end of the week the matter was, for the time being, practically forgotten.
10027{_}2465{_}CHAPTER XV.
10027{_}2466{_}THE READING-ROOM RIOT.
10027{_}2467{_}Thurston followed up his withdrawal from the football team by a number of other actions which clearly showed a determination to spend what was known to be his last term at Ronleigh in living at open enmity with those who had once been his friends and associates.
10027{_}2468{_}He never played unless it was in one of the rough-and-ready practice games, composed chiefly of stragglers, who, from being kept in and various other causes, were too late for the regular pick-ups, and came drifting on to the field later in the afternoon.
10027{_}2469{_}He severed his connection with the debating society, and shunning the society of his comrades in the Sixth, was seen more frequently than ever hobnobbing with Gull and Hawley, or lounging about in conversation with Noaks and Mouler.
10027{_}2470{_}Fletcher senior, a mean, double-faced fellow, continued, as the saying goes, "to run with the hare and hunt with the hounds."
10027{_}2471{_}"It's an awful pity about old 'Thirsty,'" he would say to his brother prefects.
10027{_}2472{_}"I try to keep him a bit straight; but upon my word, if he will go on being so friendly with such cads as Gull and Noaks, I shall chuck him altogether."
10027{_}2473{_}The speaker's methods of endeavouring to keep his chum straight were, to say the least of it, not very effective, and, if anything, rather more calculated to encourage him still further in his descent along the downward road.
10027{_}2474{_}"Look here!"
10027{_}2475{_}said Fletcher, as they sat one evening talking in Thurston's study: "don't you think you'd better make peace with Allingford and the rest, and be a nice white sheep again, instead of a giddy old black one?
10027{_}2476{_}I can tell you at present they don't look upon you as being a particular credit to the Sixth."
10027{_}2477{_}"I don't care what they think; they're a beastly set of prigs, and I'll have nothing more to do with them--with Allingford especially."
10027{_}2478{_}"Well, of course," answered Fletcher, with an air of resignation, "the quarrel's yours and not mine.
10027{_}2479{_}I must own that I think Allingford made a great deal of unnecessary fuss over that Black Swan business, and acted very shabbily in making you send in your resignation just before the holidays.
10027{_}2480{_}There's something, too, that I can't understand about the doctor's not confirming your re-election; and I think there ought to have been some further attempt made to get you to remain in the team-- you did a lot of good service last season.
10027{_}2481{_}However, my advice is, Put your pride in your pocket, and return to the fold."
10027{_}2482{_}Young Carton had shown that he possessed a certain amount of insight into character when he told Diggory that Thurston was a dangerous fellow to cross.
10027{_}2483{_}The ex-prefect's brow darkened as Fletcher enumerated this list of real or imaginary grievances, and at the conclusion of the latter's speech there was a short silence.
10027{_}2484{_}"Yes," said Thurston, suddenly making the fender jump and rattle with a vicious kick.
10027{_}2485{_}"Allingford's got his knife in me; he's bent on spoiling my life here.
10027{_}2486{_}But that's a game two can play at.
10027{_}2487{_}I've got a plan or two in my head, and I'll take the change out of him and those other prigs before the term's finished."
10027{_}2488{_}Grundy still continued to brag and swagger in the Lower Fourth, but his attitude towards Jack Vance suddenly underwent a change.
10027{_}2489{_}Towards the latter he assumed quite a friendly bearing, and though still remaining a stanch Thurstonian, refrained from making himself aggressively obnoxious to the Triple Alliance.
10027{_}2490{_}The hatchet had been buried for nearly a fortnight when an event happened which caused Ronleigh College to be once more convulsed with excitement and party feeling--a certain air of mystery which pervaded the whole affair tending to considerably increase the interest which the occurrence itself awakened.
10027{_}2491{_}Allingford had not, perhaps, been altogether wise in his choice of Lucas as keeper of the reading-room.
10027{_}2492{_}The latter was a studious, hard-working boy in the Fifth, whose parents were known to be in comparatively poor circumstances, and the captain had named him in preference to Ferris, thinking that the guinea which was given as remuneration to the holder of this post, as well as to the two librarians, would be specially acceptable to one who seldom had the means to purchase the books which he longed to possess.
10027{_}2493{_}The duties of the keeper of the reading-room were to receive and take charge of the papers and magazines, to keep the accounts, and to be nominally responsible for the order of the room.
10027{_}2494{_}I say nominally, as the law relating to absolute silence was never actually enforced; and as long as the members amused themselves in a reasonably quiet manner, and without turning the place into a bear-garden, they were allowed to converse over their games of chess or draughts, and exchange their opinions on the news of the day.
10027{_}2495{_}Lucas was, if one may say it, a little too conscientious in the execution of his duties, and rather apt to be fussy and a trifle overbearing in his manner.
10027{_}2496{_}He posted copies of the rules on each of the four walls of the room, and insisted on decorous behaviour and perfect silence.
10027{_}2497{_}The consequence was that he soon became the butt of innumerable jokes: fellows said they weren't in school, and meant to enjoy themselves.
10027{_}2498{_}"Rats" hit on the idea of carrying in an old newspaper under his coat.
10027{_}2499{_}This he surreptitiously produced, and pretended to read as though it belonged to the room.
10027{_}2500{_}At a favourable moment, with an exclamation of, "Well, this is a rotten paper!"
10027{_}2501{_}he suddenly crunched the sheet up in his hands and tore it into fifty pieces.
10027{_}2502{_}Lucas, naturally imagining that the property of the room was being destroyed, rushed up exploding with wrath.
10027{_}2503{_}An explanation followed, and the whole assembly went off into fits of merriment, at the latter's expense.
10027{_}2504{_}By the time this trick was worn out, other waggish gentlemen had introduced the practice of dropping wax matches on the floor and treading on them, and of hunting an imaginary moth--an irresistibly humorous proceeding, in which the participators rushed about brandishing books and magazines, ever and anon crying, "There he is!"
10027{_}2505{_}and smiting on the head some quiet, unoffending reader.
10027{_}2506{_}Some evil-minded young miscreant went so far as to put bits of india-rubber on the top of the stove, the consequence being that in a short time a mysterious smell arose of such a fearful and distressing nature that every one was obliged to bolt out into the passage.
10027{_}2507{_}Those boys who at the time of the elections had formed the rank and file of the Thurstonian party, saw here an opportunity for showing their resentment of what they still chose to consider unfair conduct on Allingford's part.
10027{_}2508{_}As a result, so they said, of the captain's favouritism, Lucas had been forced into a position for which he was entirely un-fitted; and with the expressed determination "not to stand him at any price," they proved themselves ever ready to assist in keeping up a constant repetition of the disturbances which have just been described.
10027{_}2509{_}These games, it need hardly be said, were not carried on when any of the prefects or members of the Sixth happened to be present; but during the half-hour between the end of tea and the commencement of preparation, when it rarely happened that any of the seniors put in an appearance, the conduct of the place went steadily from bad to worse.
10027{_}2510{_}Lucas lost his head and lost his temper, and in doing so lost all control of his charge; and at last things were brought to a climax in the manner we are about to describe.
10027{_}2511{_}At the back of the room was one of those short desks which can be changed at will into a seat, the top part falling over and making a back-rest, while the form remains stationary.
10027{_}2512{_}In connection with this article of furniture Gull one evening introduced a new pastime, which he called putting fellows in the stocks, and which consisted in decoying innocent small boys into taking a seat, then suddenly pushing them backwards on to the floor, and imprisoning their feet between the form and the reversible desk--a position from which they only extricated themselves with considerable difficulty.
10027{_}2513{_}Lucas made a couple of attempts to interfere and stop the proceedings, and when at length, for the third time, a thud and a shout of laughter announced that still another victim had fallen into the trap, he rose in wrath, and ordered Gull to leave the room.
10027{_}2514{_}"I shan't," returned the other.
10027{_}2515{_}"Keep to yourself, and mind your own business."
10027{_}2516{_}"That's just what I'm doing; you know the rules as well as I do.
10027{_}2517{_}It's my business to keep order in this room."
10027{_}2518{_}"Rubbish!
10027{_}2519{_}Who do you think cares for your rules, you jack-in-office?"
10027{_}2520{_}"Will you leave the room?"
10027{_}2521{_}"No, of course I won't.
10027{_}2522{_}If you want to act 'chucker-out,' you'd better try it on."
10027{_}2523{_}In desperation Lucas resolved to play his last card.
10027{_}2524{_}"Look here, Gull," he said, rising from his seat.
10027{_}2525{_}"You know I'm not your match in size or strength, or you wouldn't challenge me to fight; but this I will do: unless you leave the room, I shall go at once and report you to Dr.
10027{_}2526{_}Denson."
10027{_}2527{_}The offender, seeing perhaps that this was no empty threat, evidently considered it the wiser plan not to risk an interview with the head-master.
10027{_}2528{_}"Oh, keep your wig on!"
10027{_}2529{_}he answered, with a scornful laugh.
10027{_}2530{_}"I shouldn't like to make you prove yourself a sneak as well as a coward.
10027{_}2531{_}I'm going in a minute."
10027{_}2532{_}The assembly, who for the most part considered the stocks joke very good fun, and were possessed with all the traditional schoolboy hatred for anything in the shape of telling tales, showed their disapproval with a good deal of booing and hissing as Gull sauntered out of the room, and Lucas bent over his accounts with the despairing sense of having lost instead of gained by the encounter.
10027{_}2533{_}It soon became evident that the matter was not to be allowed to drop without some show of feeling, for on the following morning the unfortunate official was greeted with jeers and uncomplimentary remarks wherever he went.
10027{_}2534{_}Just before tea Diggory and Jack Vance were crossing the quadrangle on their way from the gymnasium to the schoolroom, when they were accosted by Fletcher junior.
10027{_}2535{_}"I say," remarked the latter, in rather a knowing manner, "if you want to see a lark, come to the reading-room before 'prep.'"
10027{_}2536{_}"Why, what's up?"
10027{_}2537{_}"Oh, never mind; don't tell any one I told you," and the speaker passed on.
10027{_}2538{_}"Shall we go?"
10027{_}2539{_}said Diggory.
10027{_}2540{_}"We might as well," answered his companion, laughing.
10027{_}2541{_}"I wonder what the joke is!
10027{_}2542{_}Another moth-hunt, or some more of that 'stocks' business, I suppose."
10027{_}2543{_}When the two friends entered the reading-room, it presented an unusually quiet and orderly appearance.
10027{_}2544{_}About twenty boys were seated at the various desks and tables, all occupied with games of chess or draughts, or in the perusal of magazines and papers.
